Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate

Who May Demand Hadd al-Qadhf After Death
Ruling with proof on who may demand hadd after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who may demand hadd al-qadhf after the death of the slandered person, and explain the underlying principle of the predominance of Allah's right over the individual's right.

Core Idea
Death of the slandered person voids the hadd; only father, child, and grandchild may demand it.

When a person is slandered (falsely accused of adultery), the fixed punishment (hadd) is 80 lashes. But what happens if the slandered person dies before the punishment is carried out? The Hanafi school has a detailed ruling that limits who can demand the hadd after death, based on the nature of the right involved. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the central question addressed in this passage? The passage addresses two issues: (1) who may demand the hadd when the slandered person has died, and (2) the effect of the death on the hadd itself.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Who exactly may demand the hadd after the slandered person's death? Only the father (including maternal grandfather), child (son or daughter), and grandchild (child's child) may demand. No one else, including siblings or the husband, may demand. Additionally, a child may not demand against his/her own father, and a slave may not demand against his master.
The hadd is voided by death of the slandered person, but if an eligible relative demands it, the hadd is still carried out as a punishment for the slanderer.
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
How does the school use the hadith on qisas to support its view? The hadith 'لا يقاد الولد بولده، ولا السيد بعبده' (A parent is not killed for his child, nor a master for his slave) is cited. Since qisas (retaliation) is dropped in these cases, the hadd (which is less certain) is also dropped, following the principle of a fortiori reasoning (ولأولى).
The certainty (yaqin) required for hadd is higher than for qisas, so the hadd is more deserving of being dropped when the relationship creates a legal impediment.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
What is al-Shafi'i's view and on what principle is it based? Al-Shafi'i holds that the right to demand the hadd passes to all heirs, and death does not void it. This is because he considers the predominant right in qadhf to be the right of the individual (haqq al-'abd), which is inheritable.
Malik and Ahmad agree with al-Shafi'i on this point.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference results from this ikhtilaf? Under the Hanafi view, only direct ascendants and descendants can demand the hadd after death; under the Shafi'i view, all heirs can demand it. This affects whether the punishment is carried out when the slandered person dies without such close relatives.
The difference stems from the fundamental usul principle of whether qadhf is predominantly haqq Allah or haqq al-'abd.

Source Text

ميت، فطلب الوالد أو الولد أو ولده حدّ ولا يطالب ولد أباه، وعبد سيده بقذف أمه، ويبطل بموت المقذوف . أي حد القذف = وعند الشافعي: لا يثبت هذا الحق إلا للوارث حتى لا يكون لابنه الكافر والعبد وأولاد بنته المطالبة، وهذا مبني على أن الغالب في القذف حق العبد عنده فيورث وعندنا الغالب حق الله تعالى فلا يورث فيجوز المطالبة لمن يقع القدح في نسبه بقذفه، وهو الأصل والفرع؛ لأن العار يلحقهم لمكان الجزئية. فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يكون لهم ولاية المطالبة حياً كان المقذوف أو ميتاً حاضراً كان أو غائباً وكذا إذا مات بعد القذف، قلنا: العار يلحق المقذوف قصداً وهؤلاء يلحقهم ضمناً فلا تعتبر خصومتهم ما دام حيا؛ لأن ما ثبت في ضمن غيره لا يعطى له حكم نفسه. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) ميت: محصنة أو قال: يا ابن الزاني وأبوه ميت (ط) الوالد : أي والد الأم وهو جد المقذوف وإن علا ولو كافراً أو عبداً. (ط) الولد: سواء كان ابنا أو بنتا كافرا أو مسلما حرا أو عبدا عيني) ولده: أي ولد الولد وإن سفل له حق المطالبة مع بقاء الولد. (ط) حد أي القاذف؛ لأنه قذف محصنة بعد موتها وهؤلاء مطالبة لوقوع القدح في نسبهم بقذفها. (ط، عيني) ولا يطالب ولد أباه أي لا يطالب فرع وإن سفل "أباه" أي أصله ذكراً كان أو أنثى، فدخلت أمه وجدته وإن وكذا جده وإن علا، والمنفي وجوب الحد فلا ينافي لزوم التعزير وقوله بقذف أمه أي لا يطالب الفرع أباه بقذف أمه ولا العبد مولاه بقذف أمه؛ لأنهما لا يعاقبان بسببهما حتى سقط القصاص لو قتل الأب ولده والمولى عبده، وهذا بالإجماع إلا في رواية عن مالك يطالب الولد أباه بقذف ،أمه ولو كان لها ابن من غيره له أن يطالبه لتحقق السبب، وانعدام المانع وجه سقوط القصاص بالقتل قوله عليها: "لا يقاد الولد بولده، ولا السيد ه ، فالحد أولى بالسقوط؛ لعدم التيقن بسببه. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) علت بعبده"، أمه: أي أم الولد والعبد الحرة المسلمة المحصنة. (معدن) ويبطل بموت المقذوف: أي من قذف غيره فمات المقذوف بطل الحد عندنا، وقال الشافعي لا يبطل وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو مات بعد ما أقيم بعض الحد، وهذا مبني على الأصل الذي ذكرنا من أنه يورث عنده لا عندنا ولا خلاف في أن فيه حق الشرع وحق العبد، فلما اجتمع فيه الحقان، فالشافعي مال إلى تغليب حق العبد تقديماً لحق العبد؛ لحاجته وغناء الشرع، ونحن صرنا إلى تغليب حق الشرع؛ لأن حق العبد يتولاه مولاه، فيصير العبد مرعياً به؛ ولا كذلك حق الشرع؛ لأنه لا ولاية للعبد في استيفاء حق الشرع إلا نيابة، وهو أصل مشهور مختلف بيننا وبينه يبتنى عليه كثير من مسائل الفروع.
If (the slandered person) is dead, then the father, the child, or the child's child may demand the hadd (of slander). And a child may not demand against his father, nor a slave against his master for slandering his mother. And (the hadd) becomes void by the death of the slandered person. That is, the hadd of slander. According to al-Shafi'i: this right is only established for the heir, so that his disbelieving son, the slave, and his daughter's children may not demand. This is based on the principle that in slander, the right of the slave (i.e., individual) predominates according to him, so it is inherited. According to us, the right of Allah predominates, so it is not inherited. Hence, the demand is permitted for those whose lineage is impugned by the slander—namely the root (father) and the branch (child)—because the disgrace affects them due to their connection. If it is said: they should have the authority to demand whether the slandered person is alive or dead, present or absent, and likewise if he dies after the slander, we say: the disgrace affects the slandered person directly, and these (relatives) are affected indirectly, so their litigation is not considered as long as he is alive, because what is established as subsidiary to another is not given the ruling of that other itself. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) (The slandered) dead: a chaste woman, or if he said: O son of a fornicator, and his father is dead. (T) al-Walid: the mother's father, i.e., the grandfather of the slandered person, however high, even if a disbeliever or a slave. (T) al-Walad: whether a son or daughter, disbeliever or Muslim, free or slave. ('Ayni) Waladuhu: i.e., the child's child, however low, has the right to demand while the child (the intermediate) is still alive. (T) Hadd: i.e., of the slanderer, because he slandered a chaste woman after her death, and these (relatives) demand because the slander impugns their lineage. (T, 'Ayni) And a child may not demand against his father: i.e., a descendant, however low, may not demand against his root, whether male or female, so this includes his mother and grandmother, however high, and likewise his grandfather, however high. What is negated is the obligation of the hadd, so it does not preclude the imposition of a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). And his statement: "for slandering his mother" means the descendant may not demand against his father for slandering his mother, nor the slave against his master for slandering his mother, because they are not punished on account of them, to the extent that retaliation (qisas) is dropped if the father kills his child or the master kills his slave. This is by consensus, except for a narration from Malik: the child may demand against his father for slandering his mother. If she had a son from another (man), he may demand against him because the cause is realized and the impediment is absent. The reason for dropping qisas in killing is his statement (peace be upon him): "A parent is not killed for (killing) his child, nor is a slave master killed for (killing) his slave." So the hadd (of slander) is more deserving of being dropped due to lack of certainty in its cause. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) Ummihi: i.e., the mother of the child, and the slave (is a free, Muslim, chaste woman). (Ma'din) And it becomes void by the death of the slandered person: i.e., whoever slanders another, and the slandered person dies, the hadd is void according to us. Al-Shafi'i said it is not void, and Malik and Ahmad agree. The same disagreement applies if he dies after some of the hadd has been carried out. This is based on the principle we mentioned: that it is inherited according to him, not according to us. There is no disagreement that it involves the right of Allah and the right of the individual. When both rights are combined, al-Shafi'i inclined to give precedence to the right of the individual, advancing the right of the individual due to his need and the self-sufficiency of Allah, while we incline to give precedence to the right of Allah, because the right of the individual is overseen by his guardian, so the individual is looked after by him, but not so the right of Allah, because the individual has no authority over exacting the right of Allah except as a deputy. This is a well-known principle of difference between us and him, upon which many derived issues are built.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of hadd al-qadhf (punishment for false accusation of adultery). It addresses two main issues: (1) who has standing to demand the hadd when the slandered person has died, and (2) the effect of the slandered person's death on the hadd itself. The Hanafi position is that only the lineal ascendants (father, grandfather) and descendants (son, daughter, grandchildren) of the deceased slandered person may demand the hadd. This is because the disgrace of the slander extends to them—it impugns their lineage. However, a child may not demand against his own father, nor a slave against his master, due to the principle that they are not punished for causing harm to themselves (the principle that a father is not killed for killing his son, etc.). The hadd is voided by the death of the slandered person because the predominant right in qadhf is the right of Allah (haqq Allah), not the right of the individual. The Hanafi school considers qadhf to have both divine and individual aspects, but the divine aspect governs inheritance, so the hadd does not pass to heirs. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that it is primarily a right of the individual, hence inheritable. The passage explains this foundational difference and notes that it affects many subsidiary rulings. The commentary also touches on ta'zir (discretionary punishment) when the hadd is not applicable, and cites the consensus on the dropping of qisas between father and son.

Key Terms
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed corporal punishment (80 lashes) for falsely accusing a chaste Muslim of adultery.
المقذوف (al-maqdhuuf): The person who is slandered (falsely accused of adultery).
الوالد (al-waalid): The father (or grandfather) of the slandered person, including the maternal grandfather.
الولد (al-walad): The child of the slandered person, including grandchildren.
التعزير (al-ta'zir): A discretionary punishment left to the judge's discretion for offenses without fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man slanders a woman and she dies before the hadd is carried out, who may demand the hadd?
A) Any heir of the woman
B) Only her father, child, or grandchild
C) Only her husband or father
D) The state prosecutor

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hadd al-qadhf is predominantly a right of Allah, so it is not inherited; only the father, child, and grandchild may demand it after the slandered person's death.

Retraction and Pardon Do Not Nullify Hadd al-Qadhf
Ruling on retraction and pardon in qadhf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on retraction and pardon in hadd al-qadhf and identify the intra-madhhab disagreement regarding pardon.

Core Idea
Retraction and pardon do not nullify hadd al-qadhf because the right of Allah predominates.

In cases of slander (qadhf), once a person confesses, can he retract? Can the victim pardon the slanderer and stop the punishment? This lesson explores the Hanafi position that neither action nullifies the hadd, despite some disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
This passage addresses two situations: retraction of confession by the slanderer, and pardon by the slandered person. Note the ruling for each.
The ruling is the same: the hadd is not nullified.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage states that the hadd is a right of the individual (haqq al-'abd). However, in qadhf, the right of Allah predominates, so retraction and pardon do not affect the punishment.
This distinguishes qadhf from purely financial rights.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that pardon is valid because it ends the dispute. Contrast this with the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
The majority view is the mufta bihi (operative position).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that retraction and pardon do not nullify the hadd. This is the view taken by the later Hanafi authorities.
The minority view of Abu Yusuf is noted but not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بالرجوع والعفو أي لا يبطل الحد بالرجوع عن الإقرار؛ لأنه حق العبد. والعفو: أي ولا يبطل أيضاً بالعفو عن القاذف. (مستخلص، عيني) المقذوف سواء كان قبل إقامة الحد أو في وسطه. (ط) لا بالرجوع أي لا يبطل بالرجوع عن الإقرار؛ لأنه حق العبد. (عيني) والعفو: أي ولا يبطل أيضاً بالعفو عن القاذف. (عيني) وعن أبي يوسف أن عفوه يصح؛ لانتهاء الخصومة به. (مستخلص، عيني)
(The hadd is) not (nullified) by retraction or pardon. That is, the hadd is not nullified by retraction from the confession, because it is a right of the individual. And pardon: i.e., it is also not nullified by pardoning the slanderer. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) The slandered person, whether before the hadd is carried out or during it. (T) Not by retraction: i.e., it is not nullified by retraction from the confession because it is a right of the individual. ('Ayni) And pardon: i.e., it is also not nullified by pardoning the slanderer. ('Ayni) And from Abu Yusuf: his pardon is valid because the dispute ends with it. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage states an important principle in Hanafi fiqh: once a person has confessed to slander (qadhf), his subsequent retraction is not accepted, and the hadd punishment is not waived. Similarly, the victim's (or his guardian's) pardon of the slanderer does not drop the hadd. This is because the predominant right in qadhf is the right of Allah (haqq Allah). Unlike purely personal rights (like financial claims), where the individual can forgive or retract, the hadd of qadhf involves a divine right. However, there is a transmission from Imam Abu Yusuf that the pardon of the slandered person is valid because the dispute ends with it, implying that the right of the individual is also considered. But the relied-upon position is that pardon does not nullify the hadd. The passage also clarifies that the ruling applies whether the slanderer's confession or the victim's pardon occurs before or during the execution of the hadd.

Key Terms
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Retraction by the confessor after confessing to qadhf.
العفو (al-'afw): Pardon by the slandered person of the slanderer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person confesses to slander and then retracts his confession, what happens to the hadd punishment?
A) The hadd is nullified immediately
B) The hadd is still carried out, as retraction is not accepted
C) The hadd is reduced to ta'zir
D) The hadd is postponed until proof is established

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hadd of qadhf is not nullified by the slanderer's retraction or the victim's pardon, because the predominant right is Allah's.

Linguistic Equivocation in Qadhf: The Case of 'Zanaytu'
Ruling on slander when using ambiguous words like 'zanaytu' with hamza.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on slander when an ambiguous word is used, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf from that of Muhammad al-Shaybani, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Customary understanding overrides literal meaning: slander is determined by common usage, not linguistic technicalities.

This lesson examines a subtle yet important case in the law of slander (qadhf). When someone utters a word that sounds like an accusation of adultery but claims an innocent meaning, how does the law treat his excuse? The Hanafi school contains an internal disagreement that hinges on the weight given to literal versus customary meanings. By understanding this dispute, you will grasp how the law balances linguistic precision with social context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the claimed innocent meaning
The act is uttering 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' with a hamza, which phonetically resembles the slander 'zanaytu' (I committed adultery). The speaker claims he meant 'I climbed the mountain' (literal meaning of the hamza form).
The change from soft letter (alif) to hamza is a common Arab linguistic habit.
2
State the Hanafi majority position and its reasoning
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the speaker is punished with hadd because the alteration is customary and the default understanding is slander; his claim of innocent meaning is not accepted.
They rely on 'urf (custom) as a secondary proof.
3
State the minority position and its reasoning
Muhammad al-Shaybani (and al-Shafi'i) hold that the speaker is believed and not punished because the hamza form is literally correct for climbing and he intended the literal meaning.
They prioritize linguistic fact over customary usage.
4
Identify the key point of disagreement
The dispute is whether to give precedence to literal linguistic truth or to customary understanding in a context where the word is generally used as slander.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Under the majority view, hadd is imposed; under the minority view, it is not. The mufta bihi (operative) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
This is the ruling to be given in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال زنأت في الجبل، وعنى به الصعود، حد، ولو قال: أي قصد بقوله زنات على الجبل عند الشيخين أي لا يبطل حد: أي من قال لآخر: "زنأت في الجبل" بالهمزة وقال عنيت به الصعود حد عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ولا يصدق في قصده؛ لأنه همز حرف اللين، وذا من عادة العرب، وقال محمد يصدق ولا يحد؛ لأن المهموز منه حقيقة للصعود في اللغة، وقد نوى حقيقة اللفظ، و به قال الشافعي، ولو لم يعن به الصعود يحد إجماعاً، ولو قال: زنأت على الجبل بكلمة "على"، قيل: يحد، وقيل: لا. رمز الحقائق ٣٩٥/١] (مستخلص)
And if he said, 'I climbed (zanaytu) the mountain' and meant climbing, he is punished (with hadd). And if he said—i.e., he intended by his statement 'zanaytu' (on the mountain) according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)—that it does not nullify (the hadd). Hadd: i.e., whoever says to another, 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' with a hamza and says he meant climbing it, is punished with hadd according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and he is not believed in his intention because he changed a soft letter (to a hamza), which is customary among the Arabs. Muhammad said: he is believed and not punished because the word with hamza is literarily true for climbing, and he intended the literal meaning. Al-Shafi'i agreed with Muhammad. If he did not mean climbing, he is punished by consensus. If he said, 'zanaytu 'ala al-jabal' with the word 'ala', it is said he is punished, and it is said he is not. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/395) (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where someone uses a phonetically ambiguous word that could be interpreted as slander or as something innocent. The word 'zanaytu' (without hamza) means 'I committed adultery', but 'zanaytu' with hamza (زنأت) means 'I climbed' (especially a mountain). The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if someone says 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' with a hamza, but claims he meant 'climbing', he is still liable for hadd because changing the hamza (from the soft alif to hamza) is a common Arab habit, and the default understanding is slander. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the word with hamza is literally correct for climbing, so his claim is accepted and he is not punished. Al-Shafi'i agrees with Muhammad. If he does not claim any innocent meaning, he is unanimously punished. Additionally, if the phrase is 'zanaytu 'ala al-jabal' (using 'ala instead of fi), there is a difference of opinion. This illustrates how the Hanafi school uses linguistic principles and customary usage (urf) in determining the commission of hadd offenses.

Key Terms
زنأ (zanaytu (with hamza)): To climb (a mountain).
الهمز (al-hamz): The glottal stop; changing a soft letter to a hamza.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if someone says 'zanaytu fi al-jabal' (with hamza) and claims he meant 'I climbed the mountain', what is the ruling?
A) He is believed and no hadd is imposed
B) He is punished with hadd because his claim is not accepted
C) He is given ta'zir instead of hadd
D) He is given the benefit of the doubt

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, customary usage overrides literal linguistic niceties when determining the commission of slander; a claim of innocent meaning is not accepted if the word is commonly understood as slander.

Mutual Slander: Hadd Does Not Cancel
Ruling that both parties in mutual slander are punished with hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual slander and distinguish when hadd applies versus ta'zir.

Core Idea
In mutual slander, each party is punished with hadd separately—the divine right does not cancel.

When two people accuse each other of fornication, a common misconception is that the punishments cancel out like injuries. This lesson clarifies that in Hanafi law, each slanderer is independently liable for hadd because the right of Allah predominates. We also explore the boundary between hadd and ta'zir for lesser insults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
Recognize that mutual slander (qadhf) is a fixed crime (hadd) when each party accuses the other of zina (fornication).
The act is calling someone 'fornicator' (zani) without proof.
2
Determine the ruling
Both parties are liable for hadd punishment (eighty lashes) upon their demand. The hadds are executed separately and do not cancel.
The text states 'yuhaddan kilahuma' (both are punished).
3
Understand why no cancellation
Hadd is a right of Allah (haqq Allah). Cancelling it would be dropping a divine right, which is impermissible. Qisas (retaliation) applies to personal injuries, not to hadd.
The key phrase: 'ghalib fihi haqq Allah'.
4
Differentiate hadd from ta'zir
If the mutual exchange is not a specific qadhf (e.g., general insults), no hadd applies; instead ta'zir may be given, but it cancels out unless before a judge.
Ta'zir is for individual rights (haqq adami) and can be waived.
5
Note the court context
If the dispute is before a judge, the judge may impose ta'zir on both to maintain order, even for general insults.
The exception: 'illa an yakuna bayna yaday al-qadi'.

Source Text

وعكس: أي المخاطب بأن قال: لا بل أنت زان. (عيني) حدا: يعني يحدان كلاهما؛ لأن كل واحد قاذف. (ط)] أي من قال لآخر: يا زاني فقال الآخر في جوابه: لا، بل أنت يحدان كلاهما بطلبهما ولم يلتقيا قصاصاً؛ لأن الغالب فيه حق الله فلو جعل قصاصاً يلزم إسقاط حقه تعالى وكذا لو تضاربا يعزران ولا يلتقيان قصاصاً ويبدأ بالبادي؛ لأنه أظلم، وقيد بحد القذف؛ لأنهما لو تشاتما يتكافآن ولا يعزران؛ لأن التعزير لحق الآدمي إلا أن يكون بين يدي القاضي فيعزرهما. (فتح)
And the opposite: i.e., the addressee says, 'No, rather you are a fornicator.' ('Ayni) They are both punished: i.e., both are punished, because each is a slanderer. (T) That is, whoever says to another, 'O fornicator!', and the other responds, 'No, rather you are,' both are punished upon their demand, and they do not cancel each other out (qisas) because the predominant right is Allah's right. If it were made qisas, it would necessitate dropping His right. Likewise, if they come to blows, they are given ta'zir and do not cancel each other out, and one begins with the aggressor because he is more unjust. This is restricted to the hadd of slander; if they exchange insults without slander, they cancel out and are not given ta'zir, because ta'zir is for the right of the individual, unless it is before a judge, then he gives ta'zir to both. (Fath)
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with mutual slander (qadhf). If two individuals call each other 'fornicator' (zani), each is considered a slanderer and is liable for the hadd punishment of eighty lashes. The two hadds do not cancel each other out (like qisas in bodily harm might), because the predominant right in qadhf is Allah's right, and cancelling it would mean neglecting the divine right. Similarly, if they physically fight, they are subject to ta'zir, and the one who started first is more severely punished. However, if the mutual insults do not involve a specific accusation of zina but are general profanity, then they cancel out and no ta'zir is imposed, because ta'zir in such cases is for the rights of individuals and can be waived. But if the dispute occurs before a judge, the judge may impose ta'zir on both to maintain order.

Key Terms
القصاص (al-qisas): Retaliation; here used in the sense of mutual cancellation (as in qisas for injury).
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment not fixed by shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A says to B: 'You are a fornicator.' B responds: 'No, you are a fornicator.' According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) Both are liable for hadd punishment separately.
B) Only the first speaker is liable.
C) The punishments cancel out.
D) Neither is liable because they both accused each other.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mutual slander (qadhf) results in separate hadd punishments for each party because the right of Allah is predominant and does not allow cancellation.

Reciprocal Spousal Slander: Hadd Precedes Li'an
Ruling on reciprocal spousal slander with counter-accusation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife responds to her husband's accusation of zina with a counter-accusation, and explain why the wife is punished with hadd while li'an is not performed.

Core Idea
In reciprocal spousal slander, the wife's hadd for qadhf nullifies the need for li'an.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: a husband calls his wife 'ya zaniya', and she retorts 'la bal anta' (no, you are). Intuitively, one might think both are subject to hadd or li'an. However, the Hanafi school rules that the wife is punished with the hadd of qadhf (80 lashes), and the li'an procedure is not initiated. The key is that the wife's slander triggers an immediate hadd punishment, which disqualifies her from participating in li'an, thereby rendering the li'an void. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the subtle exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband says 'ya zaniya' (O fornicatress) to his wife. She responds 'la bal anta' (no, you are the fornicator). This is a mutual slander within marriage.
The wife's response is a clear counter-accusation of zina.
2
Determine the initial ruling
According to the text, the wife is to be punished with the hadd of qadhf (80 lashes), and there is no li'an (mutual cursing).
The hadd takes precedence over li'an.
3
Understand the rationale
The wife's hadd invalidates her eligibility for li'an because a person who is punished for qadhf loses credibility (shahada). Since li'an involves swearing oaths, the wife's testimony is no longer acceptable.
The hadd is initiated first because it has the benefit of abrogating li'an.
4
Compare with the case of two strangers
If two unrelated men slander each other, both are punished with hadd regardless of who started. The order does not matter. Here, the analogy would suggest both should be punished, but the special rules of li'an modify the outcome for spouses.
The text mentions this analogy to highlight that the spousal case is exceptional.
5
Note the nuance: different interpretations of the wife's response
If the wife's 'la bal anta' could be interpreted as referring to zina before the marriage (i.e., she confirms that she committed zina before marriage but accuses him of the same), then she is confirming his accusation, so li'an is dropped and she is punished. If she meant during the marriage, then she is not confirming nor slandering, so only li'an applies.
The ruling depends on the context of the wife's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعكست أي المرأة بأن قالت: لا بل أنت. (عيني) حدت ولا لعان أي من قال لامرأته يا زانية فقالت لا بل أنت حدت المرأة فقط؛ لأن قذفها يوجب ا وقذفه يوجب اللعان فيبدأ بالحد؛ لأن في بداءته فائدة، وهو إبطال اللعان؛ لأن المحدود في القذف ليس بأهل وهو معنى قوله: "ولا لعان، ولا إبطال في عكسه أصلاً؛ لأن الملاعنة تحد حد القذف؛ لأن إحصانه لا يبطل باللعان؛ والمحدودة لا تلاعن لسقوط الشهادة به فيحتال لدفع اللعان؛ إذ هو في معنى الحد. لا يقال: قد يوجب الحد وهو قذفه لها سابقاً على قذفها له؛ لأنا نقول: لا عبرة بذلك ألا ترى أن رجلين إذا تقاذفا يحدان من غير مراعاة الترتيب ببداءة من بدأ بالقذف فهدا نظيره. (عيني، فتح) قالت: أي امرأته في جواب قوله يا زانية. (ط) بطلا : أي الحد واللعان، ولو كان ذلك أجنبية تحد. مع للقذف دونه. (ط)] لاحتمال أن تريد به قبل النكاح، فيكون ذلك تصديقاً له منها فيسقط اللعان، ويجب عليها الحد؛ لأنها قذفته، وإن تريد به حال قيام النكاح وسمته زناً للمقابلة لأجل غضبها، فلا تكون مصدقة له، ولا قاذفة، فلا يجب عليها الحد، ويجب اللعان بقذفه فوجب كل واحد من اللعان والحد في حال دون حال، =
And the opposite: i.e., the woman says, 'No, rather you are.' ('Ayni) She is punished with hadd, and there is no li'an. That is, whoever says to his wife, 'O fornicatress!' and she says, 'No, rather you are,' the woman is punished only, because her qadhf necessitates the hadd, and his qadhf necessitates li'an. So the hadd is initiated first because there is benefit in it: it invalidates the li'an, because the one who is punished for qadhf is not qualified (for li'an). This is the meaning of his statement: 'and no li'an.' And there is no invalidation in the opposite case at all, because a woman who engages in li'an is punished with the hadd of qadhf, because her ihsan is not invalidated by li'an, but a woman who has been punished with hadd does not engage in li'an due to the loss of testimony. So one resorts to averting li'an because it is in the meaning of hadd. Do not say: the hadd may be necessitated by his earlier qadhf of her before her qadhf of him; we say: no attention is paid to that. Do you not see that if two men slander each other, they are both punished without regard to order? So this is its analogy. ('Ayni, Fath) Qalat: i.e., his wife in response to his saying 'ya zaniya'. (T) Both become void: i.e., the hadd and li'an. If it were an alien (non-wife), she would be punished with hadd for the qadhf without him. (T) It is possible that she meant (the accusation) before marriage, so that would be a confirmation of him from her, thus the li'an is dropped, and the hadd becomes obligatory upon her because she slandered him. But if she meant during the marriage and called him a fornicator in response out of anger, then she is neither confirming him nor slandering him, so the hadd is not obligatory upon her, and li'an is obligatory due to his qadhf. So each of li'an and hadd becomes obligatory in different situations.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a husband accuses his wife of zina (by saying 'ya zaniya') and the wife responds with a counter-accusation ('la bal anta' - no, you are the fornicator). The ruling is nuanced. First, the wife is punished with hadd (80 lashes) for slander, and there is no li'an (mutual cursing) because the hadd punishment disqualifies the wife from participating in li'an. The husband, on the other hand, would be subject to li'an, but since the wife's hadd precedes, the li'an is not performed. However, the text also discusses another scenario: if the wife's response could be interpreted as meaning that the husband's zina occurred before their marriage, then she is actually confirming his accusation (i.e., she agrees that she herself committed zina before marriage, but then her counter-accusation is a separate slander). In that case, li'an is dropped, and the wife is punished with hadd. But if she meant the accusation during the marriage (i.e., she is calling him a fornicator now, in response to his accusation), then she is not slandering him (since it's a reciprocal accusation), so only the husband is liable for li'an, and the wife is not punished. The passage highlights the complexity of marital slander and the interplay between hadd and li'an.

Key Terms
اللعان (al-li'an): Mutual cursing between spouses when a husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses; involves oaths and invocations of curse.
الإحصان (al-ihsan): Chastity or the state of being muhsan; in the context of qadhf, it refers to the slandered person being free, adult, Muslim, and chaste.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina against a muhsan person, punishable by hadd of 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are punished with hadd
B) The wife is punished with hadd, and no li'an is performed
C) Li'an is performed, and the wife is not punished
D) Only the husband is punished with hadd

Key Takeaway
In reciprocal spousal slander, the wife's hadd for qadhf takes precedence over li'an, nullifying the need for li'an, but the interpretation of her response can alter the ruling.

Hadd al-Qadhf Not Nullified by Retraction or Forgiveness
Opening ruling on hadd al-qadhf: the penalty is not nullified by retraction or forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that hadd al-qadhf is not nullified by retraction or forgiveness, and explain its rationale based on the classification as haqq al-ʿabd.

Core Idea
Hadd al-qadhf is a right of the servant, so neither retraction nor forgiveness cancels it.

The chapter on hadd al-qadhf opens with a definitive statement: the penalty for false accusation of zina is not dropped if the accuser retracts or if the victim forgives. This may seem harsh, but it flows from the rule's nature as a right of the servant (haqq al-ʿabd), not a pure divine right. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi approach to hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is qadhf (false accusation of zina). The ruling is that the hadd (80 lashes) is a fixed penalty.
Focus on the two scenarios: retraction and forgiveness.
2
State the ruling on retraction
Retraction of the confession does not nullify the hadd because it is a right of the servant.
Contrast with pure divine rights where retraction is accepted.
3
State the ruling on forgiveness
Forgiveness by the victim also does not nullify the hadd according to the majority Hanafi position.
Abu Yusuf differs; his view is secondary.
4
Explain the rationale
The hadd is classified as haqq al-ʿabd (right of the servant), so it is not automatically nullified by the individual's actions.
In pure divine rights, retraction creates doubt and nullifies.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that forgiveness is valid because it ends the dispute, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it does not.
The cross-madhhab ikhtilaf with Shafi'i also exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حد القذف لا بالرجوع والعفو. لا يبطل الحد بالرجوع عن الإقرار; لأنه حق العبد. والعفو: ولا يبطل أيضاً بالعفو عن القاذف. فيكذب في الرجوع، بخلاف ما هو خالص حق الله تعالى؛ لأنه لا يكذب فيه، وعلى أصل الثلاثة أن ينبغي يبطل الحد بالرجوع، ومنها ما ذكره بقوله: 'والعفو' أي لا يبطل الحد بعفو المقذوف عن القاذف عندنا، وعلى أصلهم يبطل به، ومنها: أنه لا يجوز الاعتياض عنه، ويجري فيه التداخل عندنا خلافاً له، وعن أبي يوسف أن عفوه يصح؛ لانتهاء الخصومة به. المقذوف سواء كان قبل إقامة الحد أو في وسطه. لا بالرجوع أي لا يبطل بالرجوع عن الإقرار؛ لأنه حق العبد. والعفو: أي ولا يبطل أيضاً بالعفو عن القاذف.
Chapter of Hadd for Slander: It is not nullified by retraction or forgiveness. The hadd is not nullified by retraction from the confession because it is a right of the servant. And forgiveness: also it is not nullified by the forgiveness of the slandered person from the slanderer. So he is deemed truthful in retraction, unlike what is purely the right of God because he is not deemed truthful therein. And according to the view of the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), it is appropriate that hadd is nullified by retraction. And among them is what he mentioned by his saying: 'and forgiveness' i.e., hadd is not nullified by the forgiveness of the slandered person from the slanderer according to us, but according to their view it is nullified. And from them: it is not permissible to take compensation for it, and intervention (tadakhul) occurs according to us contrary to him. And from Abu Yusuf that his forgiveness is valid because the dispute ends with it. The slandered person, whether before the hadd is carried out or in the middle of it. 'No by retraction' meaning it is not nullified by retraction from the confession because it is the right of the servant. 'And forgiveness' meaning also it is not nullified by forgiveness of the slanderer.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is the opening ruling of the chapter on hadd al-qadhf (the fixed penalty for false accusation of zina). The text establishes a fundamental principle: the hadd for qadhf is a right of the servant (haqq al-ʿabd), not purely a right of God. Therefore, the penalty is not annulled if the slanderer retracts his confession, nor if the slandered person forgives the slanderer. This is contrasted with purely divine rights, where retraction is not accepted. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on the basic rule, but there is discussion: according to the view of the three, it would be appropriate for retraction to nullify the hadd, but the relied-upon position (al-madhhab) is that it does not. The commentary also notes that compensation cannot replace the hadd, and that multiple counts of slander may intervene (tadakhul). Abu Yusuf held that forgiveness after the hadd is due is valid because it ends the dispute, but this is a minority view; the school position is that forgiveness does not nullify the hadd once it has been established before the judge. The slandered person's status (free, Muslim, etc.) is relevant only for the hadd to be due, but once due, neither retraction nor forgiveness affects it.

Key Terms
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment of 80 lashes for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina.
حق العبد (haqq al-ʿabd): A legal right that belongs to the individual, such as the right to retaliation or financial compensation, which can be waived or affected by the individual's actions.
الرجوع (al-rujuʿ): Retraction by the slanderer from his confession of having made the accusation.
العفو (al-ʿafwu): Forgiveness granted by the slandered person to the slanderer after the accusation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because it is a right of the servant.
C) Because the slanderer is not truthful.
D) Because the hadd is already executed.

Key Takeaway
The hadd for qadhf is a right of the servant, so it stands regardless of retraction or forgiveness.

Ambiguous Slander Wording: The Case of 'Zanā'tu fī al-Jabal'
Dispute over ambiguous slander wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf impose hadd for ambiguous slander and evaluate the counter-argument of Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, customary usage overrides literal meaning when applying hadd penalties.

This passage presents a classic intra-madhhab dispute over ambiguous wording in slander. When someone says 'zanā'tu fī al-jabal' with a hamzah, but claims he meant 'I ascended the mountain', should he be flogged for slander? Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf say yes, because the hamzah change is a common Arab custom to hint at zina, while Imam Muhammad says no, because the word literally means 'ascend'. Understanding this dispute helps clarify the interplay between literal meaning (haqiqah) and customary usage ('urf) in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
What did the speaker say? What does he claim? What is the legal consequence (hadd) in dispute?
The act is saying 'zanā'tu fī al-jabal' with hamzah, claiming he meant 'ascend'. The dispute is whether hadd is imposed.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf)
What do Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf rule? What is their legal reasoning (talil)?
They say hadd is wajib. Their reasoning: changing the hamzah is a custom that gives the word a slanderous meaning, so the outer expression overrides the claimed intent.
3
State the opposing position (Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i)
What do Muhammad and al-Shafi'i rule? What is their proof?
They say no hadd because the word with hamzah is literally 'ascend'. The speaker intends that literal meaning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opposing argument? Which custom is cited?
The hamzah change is a known euphemism among Arabs. The outward expression is slander, so the claim of innocent intent is not accepted.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
What is the practical difference in rulings? Which position is stronger according to the text?
Practical: flogging vs no flogging. The text suggests the view of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf is stronger, and the commentary indicates it is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد: أي من قال لآخر: 'زنأت في الجبل' بالهمزة وقال عنيت به الصعود حد عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ولا يصدق في قصده؛ لأنه همز حرف اللين، وذا من عادة العرب، وقال محمد يصدق ولا يحد؛ لأن المهموز منه حقيقة للصعود في اللغة، وقد نوى حقيقة اللفظ، و به قال الشافعي، ولو لم يعن به الصعود يحد إجماعاً، ولو قال: زنأت على الجبل بكلمة 'على'، قيل: يحد، وقيل: لا.
Hadd: i.e., whoever said to another: 'zanā'tu fī al-jabal' with a hamzah and said he meant ascending, he is flogged according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, and his intent is not believed because he changed the hamzah of softness, and that is a habit of the Arabs. Muhammad said: he is believed and not flogged because the word with hamzah is a reality in the language for ascending, and he intended the real meaning of the word. Al-Shafi'i agreed. If he did not mean ascend, he is flogged by consensus. If he said: 'zanā'tu ʿalā al-jabal' with the word 'ʿalā', it is said he is flogged, and it is said he is not.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of ambiguous wording that could be construed as slander. The word 'zanā'tu' with a hamzah typically means 'I ascended' in Arabic, while without the hamzah it means 'I committed zina.' The issue is whether a person who says 'zanā'tu' intends the innocent meaning or the slanderous one. Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf hold: if he says 'zanā'tu fī al-jabal' (I ascended the mountain), he is still flogged for the outward expression, because people customarily use the hamzah of softness to hint at zina. Muhammad (Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds: the word with hamzah is literally 'ascend', so he is to be believed if he says he meant that, because he used the true literal meaning. Al-Shafi'i agrees with Muhammad. The passage also notes that if he says 'zanā'tu ʿalā al-jabal' with 'ʿalā', there is a difference of opinion: some say flogging, some say not. The point is that in assessing hadd, the outer expression (zāhir) and customary usage are given weight, but there is room for intent when the wording is genuinely ambiguous.

Key Terms
همز حرف اللين (hamz harf al-layn): A hamzah that changes the meaning of a word from an innocent action to slander, often used by Arabs as a euphemism.
حقيقة (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as used in the language.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because changing the hamzah is a common Arab custom to imply zina, so the outer expression is slander.
C) Because the speaker did not present evidence for his claim.
D) Because the word with 'fī' indicates zina.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, customary usage (ʿurf) can override literal meaning (haqiqah) when applying hudud penalties for slander.

Mutual Slander: Two Men and Husband-Wife
Rulings when two parties slander each other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for mutual slander between two men and between husband and wife, and explain why the hadd is not applied as qisas and why the wife is flogged before li'an.

Core Idea
Mutual slander does not cancel hadd penalties because the right of God is not waived by reciprocal accusation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses two common slander scenarios: two unrelated men and a married couple. The Hanafi school imposes hadd on each slanderer, rejecting any notion of qisas (cancellation). In the marital case, the wife's flogging precedes the husband's li'an, a ruling that highlights the interaction between fixed penalties and marital oaths. We will unpack the reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish the case of two unrelated men slandering each other from the case of a husband and wife slandering each other.
Both involve the phrase 'O zani/zaniyah' and a reciprocal 'No, rather you'.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For two men: both are flogged. For husband-wife: the wife is flogged, and the husband performs li'an (mutual imprecation).
The wife's hadd is carried out first.
3
Recognize the key principles
No qisas (cancellation) because the hadd is predominantly a right of God, not of the individual. Hadd cannot be set off by a reciprocal claim.
This distinguishes hadd from financial claims where mutual debt can cancel.
4
Understand the hadd-li'an interaction
In the marital case, executing the hadd on the wife makes her ineligible for li'an, thus the husband's li'an is avoided. This is because a flogged person loses the status of 'adl (integrity) required for li'an.
The priority of hadd over li'an is based on benefit: it removes the need for the oath procedure.
5
Note the irrelevance of order
The passage states that no attention is paid to who slandered first. The same logic applies as in the two-men case: both are punished irrespective of initiation.
This is confirmed by analogy: just as two men are flogged without considering order, so too in the marital case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعكس: أي المخاطب بأن قال: لا بل أنت زان. حدا: يعني يحدان كلاهما؛ لأن كل واحد قاذف. أي من قال لآخر: يا زاني فقال الآخر في جوابه: لا، بل أنت يحدان كلاهما بطلبهما ولم يلتقيا قصاصاً؛ لأن الغالب فيه حق الله فلو جعل قصاصاً يلزم إسقاط حقه تعالى وكذا لو تضاربا يعزران ولا يلتقيان قصاصاً ويبدأ بالبادي؛ لأنه أظلم، وقيد بحد القذف؛ لأنهما لو تشاتما يتكافآن ولا يعزران؛ لأن التعزير لحق الآدمي إلا أن يكون بين يدي القاضي فيعزرهما. وعكست أي المرأة بأن قالت: لا بل أنت. حدت ولا لعان أي من قال لامرأته يا زانية فقالت لا بل أنت حدت المرأة فقط؛ لأن قذفها يوجب الحد وقذفه يوجب اللعان فيبدأ بالحد؛ لأن في بداءته فائدة، وهو إبطال اللعان؛ لأن المحدود في القذف ليس بأهل وهو معنى قوله: 'ولا لعان، ولا إبطال في عكسه أصلاً؛ لأن الملاعنة تحد حد القذف؛ لأن إحصانه لا يبطل باللعان؛ والمحدودة لا تلاعن لسقوط الشهادة به فيحتال لدفع اللعان؛ إذ هو في معنى الحد. لا يقال: قد يوجب الحد وهو قذفه لها سابقاً على قذفها له؛ لأنا نقول: لا عبرة بذلك ألا ترى أن رجلين إذا تقاذفا يحدان من غير مراعاة الترتيب ببداءة من بدأ بالقذف فهدا نظيره.
And the inverse: i.e., the addressee said: 'No, rather you are a zani.' Both are flogged: meaning both are flogged because each is a slanderer. The one who said to another: 'O zani' and the other replied: 'No, rather you' are both flogged at their request, and they do not meet as qisas because the predominant element is the right of God; if it were made qisas, it would necessitate dropping God's right. Likewise, if they fought, they are punished with ta'zir and do not meet as qisas, and the one who starts is dealt with first because he is more unjust. This is restricted to hadd al-qadhf; if they exchange insults (shatama), they are equal and not punished with ta'zir unless it is before the judge, who then punishes them. And the inverse for the woman: i.e., the woman said: 'No, rather you.' The wife is flogged and there is no li'an. i.e., the one who said to his wife: 'O zaniyah' and she said: 'No, rather you' – the wife is flogged only, because her slander requires hadd and his slander requires li'an. So the hadd is carried out first because in its initiation there is a benefit: it invalidates the li'an, because the one who is flogged for slander is not eligible for li'an. This is the meaning of his saying: 'and no li'an, and there is no invalidation in the reverse at all, because the woman who undergoes li'an is flogged for slander because her ihsan is not invalidated by li'an, and the flogged woman cannot undergo li'an because of the loss of testimony. So one resorts to prevent li'an, as it is similar to hadd. It cannot be said: the hadd would be required by his earlier slander of her before her slander of him; because we say: no attention is paid to that. Do you not see that if two men slander each other, they are both flogged without consideration of order, starting with the one who initiated the slander? This is analogous.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses scenarios of mutual slander. The first scenario is two men: one calls the other 'zani' and the other replies 'no, rather you.' Both are flogged because each is a slanderer. They do not set off their hadd penalties as qisas (i.e., one cancels the other) because the right of God predominates. If they actually came to blows, they would receive ta'zir, not qisas, and the initiator is dealt with more severely. However, if they merely exchange insults (shatama) without explicit qadhf, they are not punished unless it happens before a judge. The second scenario is husband-wife: the husband calls his wife 'zaniyah' and she retorts 'no, rather you.' In this case, the wife is flogged only, because her slander of her husband requires hadd (since she is slandering him), while the husband's slander requires li'an (mutual imprecation) because he is slandering his wife. By carrying out the hadd on the wife first, the li'an is averted, because a person who is flogged for qadhf loses the eligibility to undergo li'an. The commentary clarifies that the order does not matter; the same logic applies as in the case of two men. This ruling highlights the interaction between hadd and li'an in marriage.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation or equal treatment; in slander, the idea of one penalty canceling another.
تعزير (taʿzir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd.
لعان (liʿan): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of zina without witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a case where a husband calls his wife 'zaniyah' and she replies 'no, rather you', what is the ruling?
A) Both are flogged and then undergo li'an.
B) The wife is flogged and the husband undergoes li'an.
C) Both undergo li'an.
D) Both are flogged and no li'an.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mutual slander does not set off hadd penalties; each slanderer is punished separately, and in marital cases, the wife's hadd preempts the husband's li'an.

Ambiguous Confession: The Wife's 'Zanaytu Bik' and Dropping Penalties
Ruling when wife uses first-person ambiguous statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why both hadd and li'an are dropped when a wife says 'zanaytu bik' in response to her husband's accusation, and apply the same reasoning to similar ambiguous statements.

Core Idea
Doubt (shubha) in a wife's ambiguous retort drops both hadd and li'an.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Hanafi fiqh: when a wife responds to her husband's accusation of zina by saying 'I committed zina with you,' the law suspends both the hadd for slander and the procedure of li'an. The reason is that her words can be interpreted in two ways—either as a confirmation of pre-marital zina or as a heated retort during marriage. This ambiguity creates doubt (shubha), and in Hanafi law, hadd is avoided at the slightest doubt. We will walk through the cases presented in the text and see how the principle applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary scenario
Read the initial statement: the wife says 'zanaytu bik' in response to her husband's accusation. Note that both hadd and li'an are dropped.
2
Analyze the two possible meanings
The wife could mean she committed zina before marriage (confirming his accusation) or she could be angrily retorting during marriage. Determine the legal effect of each interpretation.
If she means before marriage, she confirms his accusation, so his li'an drops and she becomes liable for qadhf (slander) against him.
3
Apply the principle of doubt
Because the meaning is ambiguous, neither interpretation can be definitively established. The Hanafi maxim 'al-hadd yudra'u bi al-shubuhat' applies: hadd penalties are repelled by doubts. Therefore both penalties are suspended.
4
Extend to similar expressions
The same ruling applies if she says 'zanaytu ma'ak' or if the wife initiates the statement before the husband's accusation.
5
Distinguish the case of strangers
When a man accuses a non-mahram woman and she responds similarly, she is flogged for slander because the possibility of pre-marital zina does not exist, so no doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قالت: زنيت بك بطلا. بطلا : أي الحد واللعان، ولو كان ذلك أجنبية تحد. للقذف دونه. لاحتمال أن تريد به قبل النكاح، فيكون ذلك تصديقاً له منها فيسقط اللعان، ويجب عليها الحد؛ لأنها قذفته، وإن تريد به حال قيام النكاح وسمته زناً للمقابلة لأجل غضبها، فلا تكون مصدقة له، ولا قاذفة، فلا يجب عليها الحد، ويجب اللعان بقذفه فوجب كل واحد من اللعان والحد في حال دون حال، فلم يجب واحد منهما بالشك فبطلا وكذا لو قالت زنيت معك؛ للاحتمالين المذكورين، وعلى هذا لو قالت ابتداء: زنيت بك ثم قذفها الرجل لا يجب على واحد منهما الحد واللعان لما ذكرنا. ولو قالت زنيت بك قبل أن أتزوجك تحد المرأة دون الرجل؛ لأن كلا منهما قذف صاحبه غير أنها صدقته فبطل موجب قذفه ولم يصدقها هو فوجب موجب قذفها، ولو قال لأجنبية يا زانية فقالت: زنيت بك تحد هي دون الرجل؛ لعدم الاحتمال الذي ذكرنا في الزوجية.
And if she said: 'I committed zina with you' both fall. Both: meaning the hadd and the li'an. If it were a stranger, she would be flogged for slander without him. Because it is possible she means before the marriage, in which case that is a confirmation of him from her, so the li'an drops and the hadd becomes obligatory on her because she slandered him. And if she meant during the marriage and is labeling him zina in response out of anger, then she is not confirming him nor is she a slanderer, so the hadd does not become obligatory on her, and the li'an becomes obligatory due to his slander. Thus each of the li'an and hadd become obligatory in a situation other than the actual, so neither is obligatory due to doubt, so both fall. Likewise if she said 'I committed zina with you' two possibilities mentioned. And based on this, if she said initially: 'I committed zina with you' and then the man slandered her, neither hadd nor li'an is obligatory on either of them. And if she said 'I committed zina with you before I married you', the woman is flogged and not the man, because both are slanderers but she confirmed him so his slander's effect is nullified, while he did not confirm her, so her slander's effect remains. And if he said to a stranger woman 'O zaniyah' and she said 'I committed zina with you', she is flogged for slander and not the man, because the possibility mentioned for spouses does not exist.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage deals with a complex scenario where the wife says 'zanaytu bik' (I committed zina with you) in response to her husband's accusation. The ruling is that both the hadd for slander and the li'an are dropped. The reason is doubt (shakk): the wife's statement could be interpreted either as a confirmation of the husband's accusation (i.e., she admits to zina before marriage) or as a retort in anger without literal meaning. Because of this ambiguity, neither punishment is applied. The same applies if she says 'zanaytu ma'ak'. If she says it before marriage, then she is confirming the accusation, so the husband's li'an drops and she is flogged for slander. If she says it during marriage and he had already accused her, then the hadd does not apply to either. The text also discusses cases involving strangers: if a man calls an unrelated woman 'zaniyah' and she responds 'zanaytu bik', she is flogged because the ambiguous possibility (of being before marriage) does not exist; she is simply slandering him. This shows how the marital relationship creates additional nuances.

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Doubt arising from ambiguous wording that prevents the application of hadd.
تحد (tuhadd): She is flogged for slander (hadd al-qadhf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are flogged for slander.
B) The husband undergoes li'an and the wife is flogged.
C) Both the hadd and li'an are dropped.
D) The wife is flogged and the husband does li'an.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a wife's statement is ambiguous, both hadd and li'an are dropped due to the principle that penalties are avoided at the slightest doubt.

Paternity Acknowledgment and Denial: Hadd and Li'an
Rulings on acknowledgment and denial of paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on acknowledgment and denial of paternity and explain why li'an or hadd applies depending on sequence, and why lineage remains established.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment fixes lineage; denial triggers li'an but does not sever it.

In this lesson, we explore a nuanced ruling that distinguishes between the legal consequences of acknowledgment and denial of paternity. The Hanafi position is that once a man acknowledges a child as his, that lineage is fixed. If he later denies it, he must perform li'an, but the child remains his. If he denies first, he incurs hadd for slander, but if he later acknowledges, the child is still his. This shows that acknowledgment overrides denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two sequences: (1) acknowledge then deny, (2) deny then acknowledge. Identify each act and the ruling that follows.
Focus on the sequence order as the key variable.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For the first scenario, the ruling is wajib al-li'an (mutual imprecation). For the second, it is wajib al-hadd (flogging for slander). Note that in both cases the child's lineage remains established.
The term 'hadd' here specifically means hadd al-qadhf (80 lashes).
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (qiyas)
In the first scenario, denial after acknowledgment constitutes qadhf (slander) against the wife, and the rule for qadhf between spouses is li'an. This is a direct qiyas. In the second scenario, the initial denial already triggered li'an, but the subsequent acknowledgment nullifies the li'an, so the original ruling of hadd for qadhf revives.
The author uses the principle that li'an is a substitute for hadd; when the substitute fails, the original applies.
4
Understand the independence of lineage from li'an
A common objection is that li'an should cut lineage. The commentator refutes this by showing that li'an can occur without cutting lineage (e.g., after a long delay) and that lineage can be cut without li'an (e.g., for a slave-girl). Thus, no necessary connection exists.
This is a key Hanafi distinctive ruling.
5
Evaluate cross-madhhab differences
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that li'an severs lineage. The Hanafi position is based on the strength of acknowledgment as a definitive proof of paternity.
The practical consequence (thamarah) affects inheritance and custody.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أقر بولد ثم نفاه يلاعنُ، وإن عكس حد، والولد له فيهما. وإن أقر بولد إلخ: لأن النسب لزم بإقراره، وبالنفي بعده صار قاذفاً، وكل قذف في قذف الزوج فيلاعن، ووجوب اللعان مقيد بما إذا لم تكن الزوجة أمة وقوله : وإن عكس حد أي حد القذف؛ لأنه لما أكذب نفسه بطل اللعان الذي كان وجب بنفي الولد؛ لأن اللعان ضروري صير إليه لضرورة التكاذب بين الزوجين، والأصل في القذف الحد، واللعان خلف عنه، فإذا بطل اللعان صير إلى الأصل. يلا عن: لأن نفي ولد امرأته يوجب اللعان ولم يوجد ما يبطل ذلك من تصديق أو تفريق. وإن عكس بأن نفاه أولاً ثم أقر بأنه ولده. والولد له فيهما: أي إذا أقر بالولد ثم نفاه أو نفاه أولاً ثم أقر به يثبت نسب الولد منه لوجود الإقرار سابقاً أو لاحقاً، فإن قيل: إذا وجب اللعان في الصورة الأولى ينبغي أن يقطع النسب قلنا: ليس من ضرورة اللعان قطع النسب؛ لأن اللعان ينفك عنه وجوداً وعدماً، ألا ترى إذا طالت المدة من حين الولادة ثم نفى يلا عن بينهما ولا يقطع النسب، ولو نفى نسب أم ولده ينتفي النسب ولا يجري اللعان فعلم أنه لا ملازمة بينهما.
And if he acknowledges a child then denies it, he performs li'an; and if he reverses, he is flogged, and the child is his in both cases. ('And if he acknowledges a child etc.': because lineage is established by his acknowledgment, and after denial he becomes a slanderer, and every slander in slandering the husband requires li'an, and the obligation of li'an is conditional upon the wife not being a slave-girl. And his saying: 'and if he reverses, he is flogged' i.e., hadd al-qadhf, because when he belied himself, the li'an which had become obligatory by denying the child is voided. Because li'an is a necessity resorted to due to the necessity of mutual denial between the spouses. The origin in slander is hadd, and li'an is a substitute for it. So when li'an becomes void, one returns to the origin. 'He performs li'an': because denying the child of his wife obligates li'an and nothing that nullifies that exists, such as affirmation or separation. 'And if he reverses': by denying first then acknowledging that it is his child. 'And the child is his in both cases': meaning if he acknowledges then denies or denies then acknowledges, the lineage of the child is established from him because the acknowledgment exists earlier or later. If it is said: If li'an becomes obligatory in the first case, it should cut off lineage. We say: It is not necessary that li'an cut off lineage, because li'an is dissociated from it in existence and non-existence. Do you not see that if a long time passes from birth, then he denies, he performs li'an between them and does not cut off lineage? And if he denies the lineage of his slave-girl's child, the lineage is cut off and li'an does not occur. So it is known there is no necessary connection between them.)
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage discusses cases involving denial of paternity. The scenario: a man initially acknowledges a child as his, then later denies it. In this case, he must perform li'an (mutual imprecation) because by denying a child he had acknowledged, he is effectively slandering his wife. The li'an is required to resolve the conflict. If he does the opposite—first denies the child, then acknowledges it—he is flogged for slander (hadd al-qadhf) because his initial denial constituted slander, and his later acknowledgment does not cancel the hadd. However, interestingly, in both cases the child's lineage (nasab) remains established to him. The commentary explains the rationale: once he acknowledges, lineage is fixed by his own admission; later denial cannot rescind it. Li'an is a procedural necessity, but it does not necessarily sever lineage. There is no necessary connection (mulazamah) between li'an and cutting lineage. Examples: if many years pass after birth, then the husband denies, he does li'an but the lineage remains. For a slave-girl, denial cuts lineage without li'an. So lineage and li'an are independent. This ruling underscores that legal parentage is determined by acknowledgment, not by subsequent actions.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity, established by acknowledgment or marriage.
لعان (liʿan): Mutual imprecation between spouses to resolve an accusation of zina and denial of paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He performs li'an, and the child remains his.
C) He performs li'an, and the child is no longer his.
D) He is flogged, and the child is his.

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment of paternity is legally binding; subsequent denial does not undo it, and the child's lineage remains established.

Denying Both Parents: No Hadd or Li'an
Ruling on a statement denying both parents' relation to the child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a statement that denies both parents' relation to a child and explain why it does not constitute slander.

Core Idea
A general denial of parentage is not slander because it rejects birth, not accuses of zina.

This lesson examines a specific utterance: a husband says to his wife about a child, 'This child is not my son nor your son.' At first glance, this might seem like an accusation of adultery, but Hanafi law draws a careful distinction. The statement denies the child's very birth from both parents, which is not an explicit charge of zina. Therefore, the severe punishments of hadd (for slander) and li'an (mutual imprecation) do not apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and context
The statement is 'this child is not my son nor your son' uttered by a husband to his wife. Recognize that it negates both paternity and maternity.
Contrast with a direct accusation like 'you have committed zina.'
2
Determine whether it constitutes qadhf
Qadhf (slander) requires an explicit accusation of zina. Here, the denial of birth is not an accusation; it is a rejection of the child's lineage from both sides.
The text says it is 'inkar al-wiladah' (denial of birth).
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
Since the statement is not an explicit accusation of zina, it does not meet the conditions for hadd or li'an. The ruling is that no punishment or li'an is required.
The same applies even if said to a stranger about his parents.
4
State the consequence
Conclude: no hadd (punishment for false accusation) and no li'an (process of mutual imprecation) are obligatory.
This is the mufta bihi position according to Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال ليس بابني ولا بابنك بطلا. ومن ليس بابني إلخ أي من قال لامرأته : هذا الولد ليس بابني ولا ابنك فلا حد ولا لعان؛ لأنه أنكر الولادة أصلاً، فيكون إنكاراً للزنا، بل هو إنكار الوطء فلا يجب بمثله حد ولا لعان؛ ولهذا لو قال لأجنبي: لست بابن فلان ولا فلانة، وهما أبواه لا يجب عليه شيء. لأن هذا إنكار الولادة فيكون إنكار للزنا؛ ولأن هذا الكلام يستعمل في نفي الخصال الحميدة والنسبة إلى الأخلاق الذميمة.
And if he said: 'He is not my son nor your son' both fall. 'And from 'not my son etc.' i.e., whoever said to his wife: 'This child is not my son nor your son' – there is no hadd and no li'an, because he denied the birth entirely, so it becomes a denial of zina, rather it is a denial of intercourse, so neither hadd nor li'an is obligatory due to it. For this reason, if he said to a stranger: 'You are not the son of so-and-so nor of so-and-so' while they are his parents, nothing is obligatory upon him. Because this is a denial of birth, so it is a denial of zina, and because this statement is used to deny praiseworthy traits and attribute to blameworthy character.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
Here a husband says to his wife about a child: 'this child is not my son nor your son.' This is a complete denial of the child's birth from both of them. Since the statement rejects the very fact of the child's birth from the marriage, it is not an accusation of zina; it is simply a denial of paternity and maternity. Therefore, no hadd for slander applies, nor is li'an required. The text explains that this kind of statement is often used rhetorically to deny good qualities or to attribute bad ones, not literally. Similarly, if someone says to a person 'you are not the son of your father and mother', it is not taken as slander but as an insult. This ruling distinguishes between an explicit accusation of zina and a general denial of parentage.

Key Terms
إنكار الولادة (inkar al-wiladah): Denial of the act of giving birth, which implicitly denies zina rather than accusing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because li'an requires the child to be living.

Key Takeaway
A denial of parentage that does not explicitly accuse of zina does not trigger hadd or li'an in Hanafi fiqh.

Exceptions to Hadd al-Qadhf: When the Victim Lacks Ihsan
Exceptions to hadd al-qadhf based on loss of ihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six cases where hadd al-qadhf is not applied according to the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle of ihsan.

Core Idea
Hadd al-qadhf is only for slandering a chaste person; any indication of zina negates the hadd.

The hadd punishment for false accusation of zina (hadd al-qadhf) protects the honor of chaste individuals. However, Hanafi fiqh recognizes that if the accuser is truthful in his accusation, or if there is strong evidence that the victim may have committed zina, the hadd is not applied. This lesson explores the six cases where the hadd is lifted, focusing on the central concept of ihsan.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Definition of Ihsan
Ihsan (chastity) is the quality that makes a person protected from slander. According to Hanafi fiqh, ihsan requires the victim to be free, Muslim, married, and chaste. Loss of any element removes this protection.
If the victim is not chaste in action or reputation, the slanderer may not be punished.
2
Identify the Six Exceptional Cases
The text lists six cases where hadd is not applied. Four are detailed: 1) a woman with a child of unknown father, 2) a woman who performed li'an due to denial of a child, 3) a man who has committed zina, 4) a man who had intercourse with a shared slave-girl. The remaining two are not elaborated but follow the same principle.
In each case, the victim's chastity is compromised by indications of zina or lack of ihsan.
3
Apply the Principle to New Scenarios
Consider a scenario not in the text: a man slanders a woman known to be promiscuous. According to the Hanafi principle, if there is evidence or strong suspicion of zina, the slanderer is not flogged. This is because the victim lacks ihsan.
The key is whether the victim is considered 'muhsan' (chaste) at the time of slander.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قذف امرأة لم يدر أبو ... لا يحد. قذف امرأة إلخ 'من' موصولة و'قذف' مع معطوفاتها صلته والجملة مبتدأ، وقوله: 'لا يحد' خبره وتشتمل على ست مسائل: الأولى: رجل قذف امرأة ومعها ولدها لم يعرف أبوه فلا يحد؛ لوجود أمارة الزنا، الثانية: رجل قذف امرأة لاعنت بولد أي بسبب نفي الولد لاعنت زوجها، وقيد بالولد؛ لأنها إذا لاعنت بغير ولد أي بقذف يحد قاذفها؛ لعدم أمارة الزنا، فلا يحد قاذف اللاعنة بنفي الولد؛ لقيام أمارة الزنا فيها، وهو ولادة الولد الذي لا أب له ففاتت العفة، والثالثة: رجل قذف رجلاً قد وطئ في غير ملكه فلا يحد؛ لفوات العفة عن المقذوف وقاذفه صادق، الرابعة: رجل وطئ أمة مشتركة بينه وبين غيره فلا يحد؛ لأن الحرمة فيها من وجه دون وجه، فينعدم العفة.
And whoever slanders a woman whose father is unknown ... he is not flogged. ('Slanders a woman etc.': 'man' is a relative pronoun and 'slanders' with its conjuncts is its predicate, and the sentence is a subject, and his saying: 'he is not flogged' is its predicate. And it includes six sub-issues: First: a man slanders a woman who has a child whose father is unknown – he is not flogged because there is an indication of zina. Second: a man slanders a woman who has performed li'an with a child, i.e., due to denial of the child she performed li'an with her husband; it is restricted to a child because if she performed li'an without a child, i.e., due to slander, then her slanderer is flogged due to lack of indication of zina. So the slanderer of a woman who performed li'an due to denial of a child is not flogged because there is an indication of zina in her, namely the birth of a child with no father, so she has lost chastity. Third: a man slanders a man who has had intercourse outside of his property – he is not flogged because the slandered has lost chastity and the slanderer is truthful. Fourth: a man has intercourse with a shared slave-girl between him and another – he is not flogged because the sanctity is from one aspect without another, so chastity is absent.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage lists exceptions to hadd al-qadhf. The text mentions that there are six sub-mas'alah, but only four are explained here. The unifying principle is that the slandered person (maqdhu) has lost the quality of ihsan (chastity) due to circumstances that suggest zina is possible. Therefore, the slanderer is not flogged. The four cases: (1) A woman with a child of unknown father – the presence of such a child indicates possible zina, so the slanderer is not punished. (2) A woman who has undergone li'an with her husband due to denial of a child – the li'an itself is an indication that she may have committed zina, so her chastity is compromised. (3) A man who has had illicit intercourse (zina) – he is not chaste, so slandering him as a zani is not punishable. (4) A man who has intercourse with a jointly owned slave-girl – since she is part-owned by him, the act is not zina, but his chastity is affected because he engaged in something questionable. These cases show that hadd al-qadhf is only for slandering a chaste person; if the victim's chastity is doubtful, the hadd is not applied.

Key Terms
إحصان (ihsan): Chastity, being free from suspicion of zina; the quality that makes slander punishable.
أمارة الزنا (amarat al-zina): An indication or evidence that zina may have occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following cases is a slanderer NOT flogged for hadd al-qadhf according to the Hanafi school?
A) Slandering a married woman with no prior indication.
B) Slandering a woman who has a child of unknown father.
C) Slandering a free, Muslim man of good character.
D) Slandering a widow.

Key Takeaway
Hadd al-qadhf is only applied when the victim is chaste (muhsan); any indication of zina or loss of ihsan removes the hadd.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Six Exemptions from Slander Punishment and the Principle of Chastity
Commentary on six scenarios exempting the slanderer from hadd and the underlying principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six cases that exempt a slanderer from hadd and apply the Hanafi principle distinguishing inherently forbidden intercourse (haram li-aynihi) from externally forbidden intercourse (haram li-ghayrihi).

Core Idea
Only inherently forbidden intercourse nullifies chastity; externally forbidden does not.

The hadd punishment for slander (qadhf) is only wajib when the slandered person is chaste (muhsan). The Hanafi school identifies six situations where the accused loses that chastity, either through clear indications of zina or through engaging in inherently unlawful intercourse. This lesson unpacks those six cases and the general principle that underlies them: the distinction between haram li-aynihi and haram li-ghayrihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six exempted scenarios
List the six cases where the slanderer is not punished: 1) woman with a child of unknown father; 2) woman who performed li'an due to a child; 3) man who had intercourse in non-owned property; 4) man who had intercourse with a jointly-owned slave-girl; 5) man who committed zina while a disbeliever; 6) mukatib who died with enough wealth for kitaba. Note the common element: each involves a loss of chastity.
The first two involve 'amarat al-zina (indication of zina), the others involve actual unlawful intercourse.
2
Observe the loss of 'ihsan'
In each case, the slandered person is not fully chaste (muhsan). For the woman with a child of unknown father, the child itself is an indication of zina. For the man who had intercourse outside his property, he clearly committed zina. Thus the slanderer's statement is truthful or the person lacks the protection of chastity.
مراعاة العفة: the passage states 'فاتت العفة' (chastity is lost).
3
Distinguish between types of prohibition
The passage draws a key distinction: if the intercourse is inherently forbidden (حرام لعينه, haram li-aynihi), the person loses ihsan, and the slanderer is not punished. If the intercourse is forbidden due to an external factor (حرام لغيره, haram li-ghayrihi), the person remains chaste, and the slanderer is punished. Examples of haram li-aynihi: zina, incest, marriage to mahram. Examples of haram li-ghayrihi: intercourse with a Magian slave-girl, or with a menstruating wife.
The passage illustrates this with the verse وَلا تَنكِحُوا مَا نَكَحَ آبَاؤُكُمْ (Quran 4:22) as an example of inherent prohibition.
4
Analyze the dalil from Quran
The Quranic verse cited (4:22) forbids marrying women married by one's father. This is an eternally binding prohibition (حرمة مؤبدة), making it inherently forbidden. Such a marriage nullifies chastity. The Hanafi school uses this to show that only inherently forbidden acts affect ihsan.
The dalil is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding the exact cause (illa).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the mukatib
Regarding a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission), there is disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (intercourse with a mukataba does not nullify chastity) and Abu Yusuf and Zufar (it does nullify chastity). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the text is that of Abu Hanifa because ownership is still established.
The doubt (shubha) in the mukatib's freedom prevents the establishment of ihsan in the first place, which is why the slanderer is not punished in that case.
