ولا يقطع بخشب، وحشيش، وقصب، وسمك، وطير، وصيد ،وزرنيخ، ومغرة، ونورة، وفاكهة رطبة، طريا كان أو مالحا بأنواعه هذه الأشياء قطعوا إلخ: أي إذا اشترك جماعة في سرقة، وأخذ المال بعضهم وقسموه بينهم، فأصاب كل واحد منهم - أي عشرة دراهم - يقطع يد الكل؛ لأن المعتاد بينهم أن يتولى بعضهم الأخذ، ويستعد الباقون للدفع، وإن أصاب لكل أقل من ذلك لا يقطع، وقال مالك: يقطعون بنصاب واحد كالقصاص، قلنا: القصاص يتعلق بسبب لا يتجزأ، وهو إزهاق الروح، فينسب إلى جميعهم بخلاف السرقة وقال :زفر إن الإخراج من الحرز يتحقق من الحامل، فيقتصر القطع عليه. ولو كان فيهم صغير أو مجنون سقط الحد عن الباقين عند الطرفين. وقال أبو يوسف: إن تولى الأخذ الصغير أو المجنون لا يجب عليهم القطع، وإن أخذ الكبار العقلاء وجب. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٢/١] نصاب وهو عشرة دراهم ولو لم يصب لكل نصاب لا قطع (عيني) بخشب : أي بسرقة خشب، والمراد بهذه "الخشب" إلى "نورة" ما يوجد تافهاً مباحاً في دار الإسلام ولا يقطع بسرقتها؛ لقول عائشة هما: من كانت اليد لا تقطع على عهد رسول الله له في الشيء التافه الحقير؛ لأن ما يوجد مباحاً غير مرغوب فيها وقلّما يأخذه أحد على كره من المالك، فلا حاجة إلى شرع الزاجر، ولأن الحرز فيها ناقص، فإن الخشب تلقى على الأبواب، والطير يطير، والصيد يفر، وهذه الصفة يورث الشبهة والحد يندرئ بها إلا أن يكون شيء من الأشياء مالاً متقوماً كالساج والآبنوس والخشب المعمول؛ لأن الصنعة تزيد قيمة على قيمة الأصل. والمراد بالطير: الطير الذي يكون صيداً، سوى الدجاج والبط عند البعض، فيجب فيهما القطع؛ لأنه بمعنى الأهلي، وقال بعضهم : لا يجب القطع في جميع الطيور، وهذا القول أصح، وكان القياس القطع في الزرنيخ؛ لأنه يحرز ويصان في دكاكين العطارين كسائر الأموال والنورة هي التي تستعمل في الحمامات لإزالة شعر البدن فلا يقطع في هذه الأشياء؛ لأنها مباحة الأصل، وعند الشافعي: يقطع بكل مال لو كان نصاباً، إلا في التراب والماء والسرقين والأشربة المطربة؛ لأنه مال محرز وبه قال مالك وأحمد وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف. (مستخلص، عيني) وحشيش أي وسرقة حشيش مطلقاً حتى الوسمة والحناء. (عيني) وقصب هو كل نبات كان ساقه أنابيب وكعوبا . (معدن) وصيد وجلود السباع وإن دبغت ما لم تجعل مصلى أو بساطاً. (فتح) ومغرة: بالفتحات الثلاث والغين المعجمة وتجوز تسكينها، وهي الطين الأحمر. (عيني) فاكهة رطبة [ أي لا يقطع في سرقة فاكهة رطبة بأنواعها، حتى العنب والرطب في الأصح. (عيني)] لأنها مما يتسارع إليه الفساد، ولذلك قال علي: "لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر"، رواه أبو داود وغيره، والكثر بفتح الكاف والثاء المثلثة: الجمار، وهو شيء أبيض لين يخرج من رأس النخلة، وهو الجذب أيضا، وفي الصحاح: الجمار شحم الحنظل، وأخطأ من قال: إنه الحطب أو صغار النحل، وقوله: "أو على شجر" أي لا قطع أيضاً في فاكهة سرقها وهي على شجر ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه أيضا، وفيه خلاف الثلاثة رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح)
And he is not cut for wood, grass, reeds, fish, birds, game, arsenic, red ochre, lime, fresh fruit, whether fresh or salted, of all their types... [repetition of joint theft]... By 'wood' is meant stealing wood, and what is intended by these 'wood' to 'lime' are things that are trivial and permissible in the land of Islam, and the hand is not cut for stealing them because of the saying of Aisha: 'The hand was not cut on the time of the Messenger of Allah for a trifling, contemptible thing.' Because what is found as permissible is not desired and rarely does anyone take it against the will of the owner, so there is no need to legislate a deterrent, and because the secure place for them is incomplete: wood is thrown at doors, birds fly, game flees. This quality gives rise to doubt, and the hadd is averted by it, unless the thing is valuable property like teak, ebony, or worked wood, because workmanship increases its value above the original. By 'birds' is meant birds that are game, excluding chickens and ducks according to some, for them cutting is obligatory because they are domestic; some say cutting is not obligatory for all birds, and this opinion is more correct. By analogy cutting would be required for arsenic because it is stored and preserved in apothecary shops like other property. Lime is that used in baths to remove body hair, so cutting is not done for these things because they are permissible in origin. According to al-Shafi'i: he is cut for any property if it reaches nisab, except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating drinks, because it is protected property. Malik and Ahmad also hold this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. Grass: including stealing grass absolutely, even woad and henna. Reeds: every plant whose stem is tubes and nodes. Game: skins of predators even if tanned, unless made into a prayer mat or carpet. Red ochre: red clay. Fresh fruit: he is not cut for stealing fresh fruit of all types, even grapes and ripe dates according to the most correct view, because it quickly spoils. Therefore Ali said: 'No cutting for fruit or palm heart,' reported by Abu Dawud and others. 'And on a tree' means there is no cutting for fruit stolen while on the tree, because it also spoils quickly, and there is disagreement among the three.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage lists specific items for which the hadd of cutting the hand is not applied under Hanafi law, even if the nisab is met. The general principle is that these items are either trifling (like wood, grass, reeds), freely available in the land of Islam (like fish, birds, game), or perishable (like fresh fruit). The primary proof is the hadith of Aisha that the hand was not cut for trifling things in the Prophet's time.

The reasoning for each item varies:
- Wood, grass, reeds: They are not usually stored securely; they are left in the open and are not highly valued.
- Fish, birds, game: They are wild or freely available; their taking is not considered theft in the same way.
- Arsenic, red ochre, lime: These are minerals or substances that are intrinsically permissible and not treated as valuable property; however, if they are processed and stored (like arsenic in shops), analogy would suggest cutting, but the Hanafi view exempts them due to their origin.
- Fresh fruit: Because it spoils quickly and is often eaten or consumed, the hadd is averted.

The passage also notes exceptions: If wood is worked (like teak or ebony) or fruit is on the tree (perishable anyway), the same ruling applies. For birds, there is a distinction between wild birds (no cutting) and domestic fowl (chickens, ducks) where cutting may apply according to some.

Finally, the text contrasts with the Shafi'i school, which generally cuts for any property reaching nisab, except for a few items like soil and water. The Hanafi position is more permissive in exempting these items due to their trivial nature and lack of secure protection.

Key Terms
خشب (khashab): Wood; here it means raw wood not processed into valuable items.
فاكهة رطبة (fakiha ratba): Fresh fruit; any fruit that spoils quickly, including grapes and ripe dates.
صيد (sayd): Game; animals that are hunted, including predators and birds.
زرنيخ (zirnikh): Arsenic; a mineral substance used in traditional medicines and dyes.
مغرة (mugra): Red ochre; a type of red clay used as pigment.
نورة (nura): Lime; quicklime used in baths for depilation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, is the hand cut for stealing fresh fruit from a tree if the fruit is worth 10 dirhams?
A) Yes, because it reaches nisab
B) No, because fresh fruit spoils quickly
C) Yes, if the fruit is on the tree
D) No, only if the fruit is dried

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts certain items from the hadd of theft due to their trifling nature, lack of secure protection, or perishability, based on a hadith from Aisha and principles of shubha.

Group Theft: Ruling on Amputation When Each Receives Nisab
Intra-Hanafi dispute on group theft amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on amputation for a group of thieves and differentiate the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Zufar.

Core Idea
For group theft, each thief must receive a nisab for amputation to apply; theft is divisible unlike qisas.

This passage tackles a common scenario: a group of thieves steals property together. Does each thief need to individually receive the minimum nisab for the hand-cutting punishment to apply? Abu Hanifa held that yes, each must get a nisab. But other Hanafi scholars like Zufar and Abu Yusuf disagreed. Understanding their proofs clarifies the school's methodology in applying hadd punishments.

Guided Path
1
State the default Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa rules that for amputation to apply to all members of a group, each must receive a nisab (ten dirhams) from the stolen goods.
This is based on the divisibility of theft – each participant can take a share.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar argues that only the thief who physically removes the goods from the secure place (hirz) should be cut. Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on capacity: if a minor or insane person takes, others are not cut; if sane adults take, all are cut.
These differences stem from how they view participation and agency.
3
Analyze the basis (dalil) for each view
Abu Hanifa: theft is divisible, so each participant's share matters separately. Zufar: the act of removal is singular, so only the carrier is responsible. Abu Yusuf: the taker's capacity affects the liability of others.
The text also contrasts with Malik's analogy to qisas, which the Hanafis reject.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and why
The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's: each must get a nisab. This is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is considered the standard.
Later Hanafi jurists have upheld this as the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآخذ بعضهم قطعوا إن أصابَ لكل نصاب، ولا يقطع بخشب، أي لو كان السراق جماعة الجمع لعلهم أي السارق الأشياء هذه قطعوا إلخ: أي إذا اشترك جماعة في سرقة، وأخذ المال بعضهم وقسموه بينهم، فأصاب كل واحد منهم - أي عشرة دراهم - يقطع يد الكل؛ لأن المعتاد بينهم أن يتولى بعضهم الأخذ، ويستعد الباقون للدفع، وإن أصاب لكل أقل من ذلك لا يقطع، وقال مالك: يقطعون بنصاب واحد كالقصاص، قلنا: القصاص يتعلق بسبب لا يتجزأ، وهو إزهاق الروح، فينسب إلى جميعهم بخلاف السرقة وقال :زفر إن الإخراج من الحرز يتحقق من الحامل، فيقتصر القطع عليه. ولو كان فيهم صغير أو مجنون سقط الحد عن الباقين عند الطرفين. وقال أبو يوسف: إن تولى الأخذ الصغير أو المجنون لا يجب عليهم القطع، وإن أخذ الكبار العقلاء وجب. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٢/١] نصاب وهو عشرة دراهم ولو لم يصب لكل نصاب لا قطع
And if some of them take, they are cut if each obtains a nisab, and he is not cut for wood, meaning if the thieves are a group ... the explanation: when a group participates in theft, and some of them take the property and divide it among themselves, then if each one gets ten dirhams, the hand of all is cut because the custom among them is that some take and others prepare to defend; if each gets less than that, no cut. Malik said: they are cut for one nisab like qisas. We said: qisas is connected to an indivisible cause, namely taking life, so it is attributed to all, unlike theft. Zufar said: the removal from the place of custody is realized by the carrier, so cutting is limited to him. If there is a minor or insane person among them, the hadd is dropped from the rest according to both sides. Abu Yusuf said: if the minor or insane person does the taking, no cutting is obligatory on them; if sane adults take, it is obligatory. [Symbol of Realities: 1/402] Nisab is ten dirhams, and if each does not get a nisab, no cut.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on a group of thieves who steal together. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is that each thief must receive a nisab (ten dirhams or equivalent) from the stolen goods for the hadd of amputation to apply to all. If only one thief physically takes the goods but they divide it, the custom is that each is considered a participant. Malik argues they should all be cut for one nisab, comparing it to qisas (retaliation) which is indivisible. The Hanafi response distinguishes qisas because it involves taking life, an indivisible act, whereas theft is divisible. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) holds that only the one who physically removes the goods from the place of custody (hirz) should be cut. Abu Yusuf differentiates based on the capacity of the taker: if a minor or insane person takes, the others are not cut; if sane adults take, they are all cut. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: each must receive a nisab for the hadd to be established. The passage also clarifies that the nisab is ten dirhams, and if the share of each is less, no amputation occurs.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold of stolen property (ten dirhams) required for the application of the hadd amputation.
حرز (hirz): The secure place of custody from which the property is stolen.
حد (hadd): A fixed, prescribed punishment under Islamic law, here amputation of the hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for amputation to apply to all members of a group of thieves?
A) The total stolen amount must be a nisab.
B) Each thief must receive a nisab from the stolen goods.
C) Only the thief who physically removes the goods is cut.
D) If any thief is a minor, no one is cut.

Key Takeaway
In group theft, the Hanafi school requires each thief to personally receive a nisab for amputation to be wajib on all.

Items Exempt from Hadd: Insignificant or with Deficient Hirz
List of items exempt from hadd with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general principle for exempting items from hadd al-sariqah due to insignificance or deficient hirz, and identify which items fall under this category, citing the hadith of Aisha.

Core Idea
Hadd is not applied for insignificant items or those with weak custody; doubt repels the punishment.

This lesson covers the first category of items for which the hand amputation is not applied in Hanafi law. The scholars rely on the hadith of Aisha and the principle that doubt repels the hadd. Items like wood, grass, game, and lime are exempt because they are either insignificant or not properly secured. However, if the item is valuable like teak or crafted wood, the ruling changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed items
Examine the passage and list the items mentioned: wood, grass, cane, birds, game, orpiment, lime, hashish, qasab, skins, and red clay.
2
Understand the reasoning
The passage cites the hadith of Aisha that the hand was not cut for insignificant items. Additionally, these items have deficient hirz (custody) because they are left unattended or are naturally mobile.
Deficient hirz creates doubt, which repels the hadd.
3
Note the exception for valuable items
If the item is valuable, such as teak, ebony, or crafted wood, the hadd applies because the value and proper custody remove the doubt.
Craftsmanship adds value.
4
Recognize intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text notes that some, including a narration from Abu Yusuf, differ on birds and orpiment. The relied-upon view is no cut for any bird and no cut for orpiment.
The hadd is avoided due to doubt.
5
Compare with Shafi'i position
Shafi'i cuts for any property reaching nisab except soil, water, dung, and intoxicating drinks. Hanafi exempts more items due to broader application of shubha.

(مستخلص، عيني) لم يحصد: أي وكذا لا يقطع في زرع؛ لعدم الإحراز. (ط) وأشربة [مطربة أو غير مطربة ولو الإناء ذهباً. (عيني، ط] أي لا يقطع في سرقة أشربة مطلقاً سواء كانت حلوة أو مرة والمراد بالأشربة الأشربة التي لا تبقى ويتسارع إليها الفساد، سواء كانت مسكرة أو غير مسكرة بخلاف الخل والعسل فإنه يقطع فيهما بالإجماع، ووجه عدم القطع في الأشربة؛ لأن السارق يتأوله على أخذها للإراقة، ولأن بعضها ليس بمال كالخمر وفي مالية بعض نقصان كالمنصف والباذق، فتحققت شبهة عدم المالية، والأشربة التي تبقى وتدخر - أي حولاً - فأكثرها مال إجماعاً فيقطع. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وطنبور : [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في طنبور وبقية آلات الملاهي. (عيني)] أي لا قطع في سرقة طنبور – بضم الطاء وكذا في طبل ودف ومزمار وبربط كما يأتي؛ لأنه يتأول السارق فيها الإنكار والأمر بالمعروف، وعند بعض الشافعية يقطع في دف وأخواته من آلات اللهو إذا بلغ نصاباً بعد نقص ماليته، ولو سرق طبلاً للغزاة، وهو يساوي عشرة تكلموا فيه، والمختار أنه لا يقطع فيه؛ لأنه كما يصلح للغزاة يصلح للهو فتمكنت الشبهة [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح) ومصحف: [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في مصحف ولو كان محلّى بحلية، ولو لم يحسن القراءة. (عيني، ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة مصحف ولو كان المصحف محلى بحلية قدر النصب، وقال الشافعي: يقطع؛ لأنه مال متقوم، حتى يجوز بيعه، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف، وعنه أيضاً أنه يقطع إذا كانت حليته نصاباً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] لأنها ليست من المصحف فتعتبر بانفرادها ولنا أن أخذ المصحف يتأول فيه القراءة والنظر، والحلية تبع له ولا عبرة للتبع كمن سرق الخمر في إناء، وقيمة الإناء مقدار النصاب أو أكثر. (مستخلص) وباب مسجد [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في باب مسجد ودار؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، ولا يقطع بمتاع الكعبة. (ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة باب مسجد؛ لعدم الإحراز، فإن قلت: الحكم في باب الدار هكذا فما الفائدة في تخصيص المسجد؟ قلت: إنما خص به لمناسبة المصحف وإنما لا يقطع بسرقة باب مركب؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، لكن لو اعتاد سرقة أبواب المساجد بولغ في تعزيره وحبسه حتى يتوب، والمراد بالمركب ما كان خارج البيت فلو كان مركباً داخل الحرز قطع. (مستخلص ، فتح) المسجد وستر
By wood: meaning theft of wood, and what is meant by these 'wood' up to 'nurah' is that which is found as insignificant and permissible in the abode of Islam, and one is not cut for stealing it due to the saying of Aisha: 'The hand was not cut during the time of the Messenger of Allah for something insignificant and contemptible.' Because what is found as permissible is not desired and rarely does anyone take it against the will of the owner, so there is no need for a deterrent law, and because the place of custody (hirz) for them is deficient, as wood is thrown on doors, birds fly, game escapes, and this characteristic creates doubt (shubha) and the hadd is repelled by it. However, if something is valuable property like teak, ebony, and crafted wood, because craftsmanship increases its value. What is meant by 'bird' is the bird that is game, excluding chickens and ducks according to some; cutting is obligatory for them because they are domesticated. Some said: cutting is not obligatory for all birds, and this is the sounder opinion. By analogy, cutting would be obligatory for orpiment (zarnikh) because it is stored and preserved in perfume shops like other properties. Nurah is that which is used in baths to remove body hair, so one is not cut for these things because they are permissible by origin. According to al-Shafi'i: he is cut for any property if it reaches nisab, except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating beverages, because it is protected property. Malik and Ahmad also said this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) And hashish: meaning theft of hashish absolutely, even wusma and hinna. ('Ayni) And qasab: it is any plant whose stem has nodes and joints. (Ma'din) And game and skins of predators even if tanned, as long as they are not made into prayer mats or rugs. (Fath) And maghrah: with three fatha and ghain mu'jamah, and it is permissible to make it sukun; it is red clay. ('Ayni) Fresh fruit [i.e., one is not cut for theft of fresh fruit of all kinds, even grapes and fresh dates in the most correct view. ('Ayni)] because it is quick to spoil, therefore Ali said: 'No cutting for fruit or kathar'; narrated by Abu Dawud and others. Al-kathar, with fatha on kaf and tha' muthallathah, is the spadix, a white soft thing that comes out of the palm tree, also called jidhb. In al-Sihah: al-jimmar is the fat of the colocynth. Whoever said it is firewood or small bees is mistaken. And His saying: 'or on a tree' means there is also no cutting for fruit stolen while on the tree, due to its quick spoilage, and there is ikhtilaf of the three [Imams]. [Symbol of Realities: 1/403] (Fath) Or on a tree, and milk, and meat, and watermelon, and uncut crops, and intoxicating beverages, and tanbur, and everything that does not last a year, and a mushaf even if ornamented, and a mosque door. Meaning: or fruit on a tree due to lack of hirz. And milk and meat: meaning no cutting for theft of milk and meat even if dried, and not for theft of any food prepared for eating like bread, and during a famine year there is no cutting for theft of food due to necessity. It is reported in the hadith of Umar: 'No cutting in a famine year, nor for a hanging date bunch.' Hence it was not detailed in al-Muntaqa between food and other things in famine, because eating is permitted without compensation in times of scarcity without distinction between prepared food and others. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) And crops: meaning no cutting for stealing uncultivated crops, restricted to uncultivated because that which is harvested and stored in non-famine years, cutting applies. According to the three [Imams]: cutting applies in all these cases. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) Not harvested: meaning similarly no cutting for crops due to lack of hirz. (T) And beverages [intoxicating or non-intoxicating even if the vessel is gold. ('Ayni, T)] meaning no cutting for theft of beverages absolutely, whether sweet or bitter; what is meant by beverages are those that do not last and spoil quickly, whether intoxicating or not, unlike vinegar and honey, for which cutting is applied by consensus. The reason for no cutting in beverages is that the thief may construe that he takes them to empty the vessel, and because some are not property like wine, and some have deficiency in value like munassif and badhiq, thus a doubt of absence of property is realized. Beverages that last and are stored for a year or more are property by consensus, so cutting applies. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) And tanbur: [similarly no cutting for tanbur and other musical instruments. ('Ayni)] meaning no cutting for theft of tanbur – with damm on ta' – and similarly for drum, duff, flute, and barbat as will follow, because the thief may construe that he is forbidding evil and enjoining good. According to some Shafi'is, cutting applies for drum and similar instruments of amusement if they reach nisab after deducting their decreased value. If one steals a drum for warriors, its value is ten dirhams, there is discussion. The chosen view is no cutting, because as it is suitable for war, it is suitable for amusement, so doubt attaches. [Symbol of Realities: 1/403] (Fath) And mushaf: [similarly no cutting for mushaf even if ornamented with a decorative binding, even if he cannot read. ('Ayni, T)] meaning no cutting for theft of a mushaf even if the mushaf is ornamented with a binding worth a nisab. Al-Shafi'i said: cutting applies because it is valuable property, as it is permissible to sell it. Malik and Ahmad also said this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. According to him, cutting applies if the ornamentation alone is a nisab. [Symbol of Realities: 1/404] because it is not part of the mushaf, so it is considered independently. Our argument: the taking of a mushaf can be construed as wanting to read or look at it, and the ornamentation is subsidiary to it, and the subsidiary is not considered, like one who steals wine in a vessel where the vessel's value is a nisab or more. (Mukhtasar) And a mosque door: [similarly no cutting for a mosque door or a house door, because it is a place of custody but without a custodian, and no cutting for the furnishings of the Kaaba. (T)] meaning no cutting for theft of a mosque door due to lack of proper custody. If you say: the ruling for a house door is the same, so what is the benefit of specifying the mosque? I say: it is specified to match the mushaf, and similarly no cutting for a door that is set up [outside the house] because it is a place of custody without a custodian. But if someone habitually steals mosque doors, he is severely reprimanded and imprisoned until he repents. What is meant by 'set up' is that which is outside the house; if it is set up inside the place of custody, cutting applies. (Mukhtasar, Fath) The mosque and curtain?
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage enumerates items for which the hadd of amputation is not applied in Hanafi law, along with the reasons and occasional madhhab differences. The general principle is that amputation is avoided when there is doubt (shubha) or when the item is not considered fully protected property. The list includes:
- Wood, grass, cane, fish, birds, game, orpiment, red ochre, lime: These are considered low-value or naturally abundant items that are not properly guarded. The hadith of Aisha is cited that the hand was not cut during the Prophet's time for insignificant items. Additionally, the place of custody (hirz) for these is weak; wood is left at doorsteps, birds fly away, etc. However, if the item is valuable (e.g., teak, ebony, or crafted wood), amputation applies.
- Birds: The text distinguishes between wild birds (no cut) and domesticated fowl like chickens and ducks (cut). Some scholars say no cut for any bird.
- Orpiment (zarnikh): By qiyas, one would cut, but the text notes it is stored properly, so it seems contradictory. It may be that the text is quoting a position, but the relied-upon view is no cut.
- Lime (nurah): Used in baths, considered permissible and not valuable.
- Fresh fruit: No cut because it spoils quickly, citing the hadith of Ali: 'No cutting for fruit or kathar' (the spadix of the palm).
- Fruit on the tree: Also no cut due to rapid spoilage.
- Milk, meat, watermelon: No cut because they perish quickly and are often not properly stored.
- Unharvested crops: No cut because they are not in proper custody.
- Intoxicating beverages: No cut because the thief may intend to discard them, and they have doubtful monetary value. If the beverage is vinegar or honey (which last), cutting applies by consensus.
- Musical instruments (tanbur, drum, etc.): No cut because the thief may claim he is forbidding evil. There is a discussion about a war drum; the chosen view is no cut due to doubt.
- Mushaf (copy of the Quran): No cut, even if ornamented, because the thief may claim he intended to read. Shafi'i differs, considering it valuable property. The Hanafi view is that the ornamentation is subsidiary to the Quranic text.
- Mosque door: No cut because the mosque is a place of custody without a specific guard. However, if someone habitually steals them, he is punished by ta'zir.
The passage also notes the Shafi'i position that cutting applies to any property of nisab except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating drinks. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the hadd is avoided in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
حرز (hirz): The secure place of custody of property; deficient hirz leads to dropping the hadd.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that repels the hadd punishment.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and of value; items that are not mal mutaqawwim do not trigger the hadd.
تافه (tafih): Insignificant, trivial; used in the hadith to describe items for which the hand is not cut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited for exempting insignificant items from hadd?
A) The hadith of Aisha
B) The hadith of Ali
C) The hadith of Umar
D) The principle of qiyas

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts items that are insignificant or have deficient hirz because doubt repels the hadd.

Items Exempt from Hadd: Perishable and Rapidly Spoiling Items
List of perishable items exempt from hadd with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not amputate for theft of perishable items such as fresh fruit, milk, meat, and unharvested crops, citing the hadith of Ali and the concept of rapid spoilage.

Core Idea
Hadd is not applied for items that spoil quickly because the hirz is inherently weak and there is doubt about true ownership.

This lesson covers items that spoil rapidly, such as fresh fruit, milk, meat, and unharvested crops. The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Ali: 'No cutting for fruit or kathar' (spadix of palm). The reasoning is that these items are not securely stored because they deteriorate quickly, and the thief may have taken them with the intention of immediate consumption or due to necessity. There is significant inter-madhhab disagreement on these items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the perishable items
List items mentioned: fresh fruit, fruit on tree, milk, meat, watermelon, unharvested crops, and intoxicating beverages. Also note the general category 'everything that does not last a year'.
2
Understand the reasoning
The passage states that these items are quick to spoil (tasarru' al-fasad). This means they are not usually stored securely, and the thief may claim he took them for immediate use or to avoid waste. The hadith of Ali supports this.
The hadith of Ali is cited as evidence.
3
Note exceptions
Items that last (like vinegar and honey) are subject to hadd by consensus. Also, once crops are harvested and stored, hadd applies. During famine, theft of food is exempt due to necessity.
4
Recognize inter-madhhab ikhtilaf
The three imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) cut for all these items if nisab is reached, arguing they are property. Hanafi exempts due to the nature of spoilage.

بخشب : أي بسرقة خشب، والمراد بهذه "الخشب" إلى "نورة" ما يوجد تافهاً مباحاً في دار الإسلام ولا يقطع بسرقتها؛ لقول عائشة هما: من كانت اليد لا تقطع على عهد رسول الله له في الشيء التافه الحقير؛ لأن ما يوجد مباحاً غير مرغوب فيها وقلّما يأخذه أحد على كره من المالك، فلا حاجة إلى شرع الزاجر، ولأن الحرز فيها ناقص، فإن الخشب تلقى على الأبواب، والطير يطير، والصيد يفر، وهذه الصفة يورث الشبهة والحد يندرئ بها إلا أن يكون شيء من الأشياء مالاً متقوماً كالساج والآبنوس والخشب المعمول؛ لأن الصنعة تزيد قيمة على قيمة الأصل. والمراد بالطير: الطير الذي يكون صيداً، سوى الدجاج والبط عند البعض، فيجب فيهما القطع؛ لأنه بمعنى الأهلي، وقال بعضهم : لا يجب القطع في جميع الطيور، وهذا القول أصح، وكان القياس القطع في الزرنيخ؛ لأنه يحرز ويصان في دكاكين العطارين كسائر الأموال والنورة هي التي تستعمل في الحمامات لإزالة شعر البدن فلا يقطع في هذه الأشياء؛ لأنها مباحة الأصل، وعند الشافعي: يقطع بكل مال لو كان نصاباً، إلا في التراب والماء والسرقين والأشربة المطربة؛ لأنه مال محرز وبه قال مالك وأحمد وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف. (مستخلص، عيني) وحشيش أي وسرقة حشيش مطلقاً حتى الوسمة والحناء. (عيني) وقصب هو كل نبات كان ساقه أنابيب وكعوبا . (معدن) وصيد وجلود السباع وإن دبغت ما لم تجعل مصلى أو بساطاً. (فتح) ومغرة: بالفتحات الثلاث والغين المعجمة وتجوز تسكينها، وهي الطين الأحمر. (عيني) فاكهة رطبة [ أي لا يقطع في سرقة فاكهة رطبة بأنواعها، حتى العنب والرطب في الأصح. (عيني)] لأنها مما يتسارع إليه الفساد، ولذلك قال علي: "لا قطع في ثمر ولا كثر"، رواه أبو داود وغيره، والكثر بفتح الكاف والثاء المثلثة: الجمار، وهو شيء أبيض لين يخرج من رأس النخلة، وهو الجذب أيضا، وفي الصحاح: الجمار شحم الحنظل، وأخطأ من قال: إنه الحطب أو صغار النحل، وقوله: "أو على شجر" أي لا قطع أيضاً في فاكهة سرقها وهي على شجر ؛ لتسارع الفساد إليه أيضا، وفيه خلاف الثلاثة رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح) أو على شجر، ولبن، ولحم، وبطيخ، وزرع لم يُحصد، وأشربة مسكرة، وطنبور، وكل ما لا يبقي حولاً ومصحف ولو محلّى، وباب مسجد، أي أو فاكهة على شجر لعدم الحرز ولبن ولحم أي ولا يقطع في سرقة لبن ولحم ولو قديداً، ولا في رقة كل مهيأ لأكل كخبز، وفي أيام قحط لا قطع بسرقة الطعام للضرورة، وقد جاء في حديث عمر ه: "لا قطع في عام سنة، ولا في عذق معلق"، ولهذا لم يفصل في "المنتقى" بين الطعام وغيره في القحط؛ لأنه يرخص في الأكل بغير القيمة في أيام المخمصة من غير تفصيل بين المهيأ للأكل وغيره. (مستخلص فتح عيني) وزرع أي ولا يقطع بسرقة زرع لم يحصد، وقيد بعدم الحصاد؛ لأن الذي حصد وأحرز في غير أيام القحط يقطع فيه، وعند الثلاثة: يقطع في هذه الصور كلها. (مستخلص، عيني) لم يحصد: أي وكذا لا يقطع في زرع؛ لعدم الإحراز. (ط) وأشربة [مطربة أو غير مطربة ولو الإناء ذهباً. (عيني، ط] أي لا يقطع في سرقة أشربة مطلقاً سواء كانت حلوة أو مرة والمراد بالأشربة الأشربة التي لا تبقى ويتسارع إليها الفساد، سواء كانت مسكرة أو غير مسكرة بخلاف الخل والعسل فإنه يقطع فيهما بالإجماع، ووجه عدم القطع في الأشربة؛ لأن السارق يتأوله على أخذها للإراقة، ولأن بعضها ليس بمال كالخمر وفي مالية بعض نقصان كالمنصف والباذق، فتحققت شبهة عدم المالية، والأشربة التي تبقى وتدخر - أي حولاً - فأكثرها مال إجماعاً فيقطع. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وطنبور : [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في طنبور وبقية آلات الملاهي. (عيني)] أي لا قطع في سرقة طنبور – بضم الطاء وكذا في طبل ودف ومزمار وبربط كما يأتي؛ لأنه يتأول السارق فيها الإنكار والأمر بالمعروف، وعند بعض الشافعية يقطع في دف وأخواته من آلات اللهو إذا بلغ نصاباً بعد نقص ماليته، ولو سرق طبلاً للغزاة، وهو يساوي عشرة تكلموا فيه، والمختار أنه لا يقطع فيه؛ لأنه كما يصلح للغزاة يصلح للهو فتمكنت الشبهة [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح) ومصحف: [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في مصحف ولو كان محلّى بحلية، ولو لم يحسن القراءة. (عيني، ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة مصحف ولو كان المصحف محلى بحلية قدر النصب، وقال الشافعي: يقطع؛ لأنه مال متقوم، حتى يجوز بيعه، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف، وعنه أيضاً أنه يقطع إذا كانت حليته نصاباً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] لأنها ليست من المصحف فتعتبر بانفرادها ولنا أن أخذ المصحف يتأول فيه القراءة والنظر، والحلية تبع له ولا عبرة للتبع كمن سرق الخمر في إناء، وقيمة الإناء مقدار النصاب أو أكثر. (مستخلص) وباب مسجد [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في باب مسجد ودار؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، ولا يقطع بمتاع الكعبة. (ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة باب مسجد؛ لعدم الإحراز، فإن قلت: الحكم في باب الدار هكذا فما الفائدة في تخصيص المسجد؟ قلت: إنما خص به لمناسبة المصحف وإنما لا يقطع بسرقة باب مركب؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، لكن لو اعتاد سرقة أبواب المساجد بولغ في تعزيره وحبسه حتى يتوب، والمراد بالمركب ما كان خارج البيت فلو كان مركباً داخل الحرز قطع. Meaning: or fruit on a tree due to lack of hirz. And milk and meat: meaning no cutting for theft of milk and meat even if dried, and not for theft of any food prepared for eating like bread, and during a famine year there is no cutting for theft of food due to necessity. It is reported in the hadith of Umar: 'No cutting in a famine year, nor for a hanging date bunch.' Hence it was not detailed in al-Muntaqa between food and other things in famine, because eating is permitted without compensation in times of scarcity without distinction between prepared food and others. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) And crops: meaning no cutting for stealing uncultivated crops, restricted to uncultivated because that which is harvested and stored in non-famine years, cutting applies. According to the three [Imams]: cutting applies in all these cases. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) Not harvested: meaning similarly no cutting for crops due to lack of hirz. (T) And beverages [intoxicating or non-intoxicating even if the vessel is gold. ('Ayni, T)] meaning no cutting for theft of beverages absolutely, whether sweet or bitter; what is meant by beverages are those that do not last and spoil quickly, whether intoxicating or not, unlike vinegar and honey, for which cutting is applied by consensus. The reason for no cutting in beverages is that the thief may construe that he takes them to empty the vessel, and because some are not property like wine, and some have deficiency in value like munassif and badhiq, thus a doubt of absence of property is realized. Beverages that last and are stored for a year or more are property by consensus, so cutting applies. (Mukhtasar, Fath, 'Ayni) And tanbur: [similarly no cutting for tanbur and other musical instruments. ('Ayni)] meaning no cutting for theft of tanbur – with damm on ta' – and similarly for drum, duff, flute, and barbat as will follow, because the thief may construe that he is forbidding evil and enjoining good. According to some Shafi'is, cutting applies for drum and similar instruments of amusement if they reach nisab after deducting their decreased value. If one steals a drum for warriors, its value is ten dirhams, there is discussion. The chosen view is no cutting, because as it is suitable for war, it is suitable for amusement, so doubt attaches. [Symbol of Realities: 1/403] (Fath) And mushaf: [similarly no cutting for mushaf even if ornamented with a decorative binding, even if he cannot read. ('Ayni, T)] meaning no cutting for theft of a mushaf even if the mushaf is ornamented with a binding worth a nisab. Al-Shafi'i said: cutting applies because it is valuable property, as it is permissible to sell it. Malik and Ahmad also said this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. According to him, cutting applies if the ornamentation alone is a nisab. [Symbol of Realities: 1/404] because it is not part of the mushaf, so it is considered independently. Our argument: the taking of a mushaf can be construed as wanting to read or look at it, and the ornamentation is subsidiary to it, and the subsidiary is not considered, like one who steals wine in a vessel where the vessel's value is a nisab or more. (Mukhtasar) And a mosque door: [similarly no cutting for a mosque door or a house door, because it is a place of custody but without a custodian, and no cutting for the furnishings of the Kaaba. (T)] meaning no cutting for theft of a mosque door due to lack of proper custody. If you say: the ruling for a house door is the same, so what is the benefit of specifying the mosque? I say: it is specified to match the mushaf, and similarly no cutting for a door that is set up [outside the house] because it is a place of custody without a custodian. But if someone habitually steals mosque doors, he is severely reprimanded and imprisoned until he repents. What is meant by 'set up' is that which is outside the house; if it is set up inside the place of custody, cutting applies. (Mukhtasar, Fath) The mosque and curtain?
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage enumerates items for which the hadd of amputation is not applied in Hanafi law, along with the reasons and occasional madhhab differences. The general principle is that amputation is avoided when there is doubt (shubha) or when the item is not considered fully protected property. The list includes:
- Wood, grass, cane, fish, birds, game, orpiment, red ochre, lime: These are considered low-value or naturally abundant items that are not properly guarded. The hadith of Aisha is cited that the hand was not cut during the Prophet's time for insignificant items. Additionally, the place of custody (hirz) for these is weak; wood is left at doorsteps, birds fly away, etc. However, if the item is valuable (e.g., teak, ebony, or crafted wood), amputation applies.
- Birds: The text distinguishes between wild birds (no cut) and domesticated fowl like chickens and ducks (cut). Some scholars say no cut for any bird.
- Orpiment (zarnikh): By qiyas, one would cut, but the text notes it is stored properly, so it seems contradictory. It may be that the text is quoting a position, but the relied-upon view is no cut.
- Lime (nurah): Used in baths, considered permissible and not valuable.
- Fresh fruit: No cut because it spoils quickly, citing the hadith of Ali: 'No cutting for fruit or kathar' (the spadix of the palm).
- Fruit on the tree: Also no cut due to rapid spoilage.
- Milk, meat, watermelon: No cut because they perish quickly and are often not properly stored.
- Unharvested crops: No cut because they are not in proper custody.
- Intoxicating beverages: No cut because the thief may intend to discard them, and they have doubtful monetary value. If the beverage is vinegar or honey (which last), cutting applies by consensus.
- Musical instruments (tanbur, drum, etc.): No cut because the thief may claim he is forbidding evil. There is a discussion about a war drum; the chosen view is no cut due to doubt.
- Mushaf (copy of the Quran): No cut, even if ornamented, because the thief may claim he intended to read. Shafi'i differs, considering it valuable property. The Hanafi view is that the ornamentation is subsidiary to the Quranic text.
- Mosque door: No cut because the mosque is a place of custody without a specific guard. However, if someone habitually steals them, he is punished by ta'zir.
The passage also notes the Shafi'i position that cutting applies to any property of nisab except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating drinks. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the hadd is avoided in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
تسارع الفساد (tasarru' al-fasad): Rapid spoilage; a characteristic that weakens hirz and creates doubt.
كثر (kathar): The spadix of the palm tree, a white soft core.
عام سنة (aam sanah): A famine year; during such times, theft of food is exempt due to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason for exempting fresh fruit from hadd?
A) It is not considered property.
B) It spoils quickly, creating doubt.
C) It is usually not guarded.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Perishable items are exempt from hadd due to rapid spoilage and the resulting doubt about custody and intent.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
6 min
Items Exempt from Hadd: Shubha of Intention and Custody
List of items exempt due to shubha in intention or custody.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school exempts items like musical instruments, mushaf, and mosque doors from hadd due to shubha of intention or lack of proper hirz, and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute regarding the mushaf.

Core Idea
Hadd is not applied when the thief can plausibly claim a lawful intention, such as reading a mushaf or forbidding evil with musical instruments.

This lesson covers items exempted because the thief may have a legitimate purpose other than theft, creating legal doubt (shubha). Musical instruments, a mushaf (copy of the Quran), and a mosque door are examples. The Hanafi school argues that the thief may intend to read the mushaf, or to break a musical instrument in forbidding evil, or the door is not in a proper custody. There is a significant inter-madhhab dispute, especially regarding the mushaf, where Shafi'i and others apply hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and the shubha
List items: tanbur (and other musical instruments), mushaf (even ornamented), mosque door, and everything that does not last a year (general perishable). The shubha for tanbur is that the thief may intend to forbid evil; for mushaf, to read or look; for mosque door, lack of custodian.
2
Understand the reasoning for each
For tanbur: the thief can claim he was going to break it as an act of enjoining good. For mushaf: he can claim he wanted to read it. For mosque door: the mosque is a place of custody but without a specific guard, so the door is not secured.
The hadd is repelled by these plausible intentions.
3
Analyze the dispute on mushaf
Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad, and a narration from Abu Yusuf hold that the mushaf is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and can be sold, so hadd applies if nisab. Hanafi responds that the subsidiary (ornamentation) follows the primary (text), and the intention is doubtful.
The comparison with stealing wine in a valuable vessel is made.
4
Note the exception for habitual theft
If someone habitually steals mosque doors, he is severely punished by ta'zir, though still no hadd.
The hadd is not applied, but the judge can impose a deterrent punishment.

بخشب : أي بسرقة خشب، والمراد بهذه "الخشب" إلى "نورة" ما يوجد تافهاً مباحاً في دار الإسلام ولا يقطع بسرقتها؛ لقول عائشة هما: من كانت اليد لا تقطع على عهد رسول الله له في الشيء التافه الحقير؛ لأن ما يوجد مباحاً غير مرغوب فيها وقلّما يأخذه أحد على كره من المالك، فلا حاجة إلى شرع الزاجر، ولأن الحرز فيها ناقص، فإن الخشب تلقى على الأبواب، والطير يطير، والصيد يفر، وهذه الصفة يورث الشبهة والحد يندرئ بها إلا أن يكون شيء من الأشياء مالاً متقوماً كالساج والآبنوس والخشب المعمول؛ لأن الصنعة تزيد قيمة على قيمة الأصل. والمراد بالطير: الطير الذي يكون صيداً، سوى الدجاج والبط عند البعض، فيجب فيهما القطع؛ لأنه بمعنى الأهلي، وقال بعضهم : لا يجب القطع في جميع الطيور، وهذا القول أصح، وكان القياس القطع في الزرنيخ؛ لأنه يحرز ويصان في دكاكين العطارين كسائر الأموال والنورة هي التي تستعمل في الحمامات لإزالة شعر البدن فلا يقطع في هذه الأشياء؛ لأنها مباحة الأصل، وعند الشافعي: يقطع بكل مال لو كان نصاباً، إلا في التراب والماء والسرقين والأشربة المطربة؛ لأنه مال محرز وبه قال مالك وأحمد وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف. (مستخلص فتح عيني) وزرع أي ولا يقطع بسرقة زرع لم يحصد، وقيد بعدم الحصاد؛ لأن الذي حصد وأحرز في غير أيام القحط يقطع فيه، وعند الثلاثة: يقطع في هذه الصور كلها. (مستخلص، عيني) لم يحصد: أي وكذا لا يقطع في زرع؛ لعدم الإحراز. (ط) وأشربة [مطربة أو غير مطربة ولو الإناء ذهباً. (عيني، ط] أي لا يقطع في سرقة أشربة مطلقاً سواء كانت حلوة أو مرة والمراد بالأشربة الأشربة التي لا تبقى ويتسارع إليها الفساد، سواء كانت مسكرة أو غير مسكرة بخلاف الخل والعسل فإنه يقطع فيهما بالإجماع، ووجه عدم القطع في الأشربة؛ لأن السارق يتأوله على أخذها للإراقة، ولأن بعضها ليس بمال كالخمر وفي مالية بعض نقصان كالمنصف والباذق، فتحققت شبهة عدم المالية، والأشربة التي تبقى وتدخر - أي حولاً - فأكثرها مال إجماعاً فيقطع. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وطنبور : [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في طنبور وبقية آلات الملاهي. (عيني)] أي لا قطع في سرقة طنبور – بضم الطاء وكذا في طبل ودف ومزمار وبربط كما يأتي؛ لأنه يتأول السارق فيها الإنكار والأمر بالمعروف، وعند بعض الشافعية يقطع في دف وأخواته من آلات اللهو إذا بلغ نصاباً بعد نقص ماليته، ولو سرق طبلاً للغزاة، وهو يساوي عشرة تكلموا فيه، والمختار أنه لا يقطع فيه؛ لأنه كما يصلح للغزاة يصلح للهو فتمكنت الشبهة [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح) ومصحف: [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في مصحف ولو كان محلّى بحلية، ولو لم يحسن القراءة. (عيني، ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة مصحف ولو كان المصحف محلى بحلية قدر النصب، وقال الشافعي: يقطع؛ لأنه مال متقوم، حتى يجوز بيعه، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف، وعنه أيضاً أنه يقطع إذا كانت حليته نصاباً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] لأنها ليست من المصحف فتعتبر بانفرادها ولنا أن أخذ المصحف يتأول فيه القراءة والنظر، والحلية تبع له ولا عبرة للتبع كمن سرق الخمر في إناء، وقيمة الإناء مقدار النصاب أو أكثر. (مستخلص) وباب مسجد [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في باب مسجد ودار؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، ولا يقطع بمتاع الكعبة. (ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة باب مسجد؛ لعدم الإحراز، فإن قلت: الحكم في باب الدار هكذا فما الفائدة في تخصيص المسجد؟ قلت: إنما خص به لمناسبة المصحف وإنما لا يقطع بسرقة باب مركب؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، لكن لو اعتاد سرقة أبواب المساجد بولغ في تعزيره وحبسه حتى يتوب، والمراد بالمركب ما كان خارج البيت فلو كان مركباً داخل الحرز قطع. (مستخلص ، فتح) المسجد وستر
By wood: meaning theft of wood, and what is meant by these 'wood' up to 'nurah' is that which is found as insignificant and permissible in the abode of Islam, and one is not cut for stealing it due to the saying of Aisha: 'The hand was not cut during the time of the Messenger of Allah for something insignificant and contemptible.' Because what is found as permissible is not desired and rarely does anyone take it against the will of the owner, so there is no need for a deterrent law, and because the place of custody (hirz) for them is deficient, as wood is thrown on doors, birds fly, game escapes, and this characteristic creates doubt (shubha) and the hadd is repelled by it. However, if something is valuable property like teak, ebony, and crafted wood, because craftsmanship increases its value. What is meant by 'bird' is the bird that is game, excluding chickens and ducks according to some; cutting is obligatory for them because they are domesticated. Some said: cutting is not obligatory for all birds, and this is the sounder opinion. By analogy, cutting would be obligatory for orpiment (zarnikh) because it is stored and preserved in perfume shops like other properties. Nurah is that which is used in baths to remove body hair, so one is not cut for these things because they are permissible by origin. According to al-Shafi'i: he is cut for any property if it reaches nisab, except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating beverages, because it is protected property. Malik and Ahmad also said this, and it is a narration from Abu Yusuf. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni) And hashish: meaning theft of hashish absolutely, even wusma and hinna. ('Ayni) And qasab: it is any plant whose stem has nodes and joints. (Ma'din) And game and skins of predators even if tanned, as long as they are not made into prayer mats or rugs. (Fath) And maghrah: with three fatha and ghain mu'jamah, and it is permissible to make it sukun; it is red clay. ('Ayni) Fresh fruit [i.e., one is not cut for theft of fresh fruit of all kinds, even grapes and fresh dates in the most correct view. ('Ayni)] because it is quick to spoil, therefore Ali said: 'No cutting for fruit or kathar'; narrated by Abu Dawud and others. Al-kathar, with fatha on kaf and tha' muthallathah, is the spadix, a white soft thing that comes out of the palm tree, also called jidhb. In al-Sihah: al-jimmar is the fat of the colocynth. Whoever said it is firewood or small bees is mistaken. And His saying: 'or on a tree' means there is also no cutting for fruit stolen while on the tree, due to its quick spoilage, and there is ikhtilaf of the three [Imams]. The passage also notes the Shafi'i position that cutting applies to any property of nisab except for soil, water, dung, and intoxicating drinks. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the hadd is avoided in cases of doubt.

Key Terms
طنبور (tanbur): A stringed musical instrument; exempt due to shubha of forbidding evil.
مصحف (mushaf): A copy of the Quran; exempt even if ornamented.
حرز لا محرز (hariz la muhriz): A place of custody without a custodian; such as a mosque door.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the hand not cut for stealing a mushaf with a valuable ornamentation?
A) Because the ornamentation is not allowed to be sold.
B) Because the thief may claim he intended to read the Quran.
C) Because the mushaf is not considered property.
D) Because the ornamentation is part of the mushaf and cannot be separated.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts items when the thief has a plausible lawful intention, such as reading the Quran or forbidding evil, even if the item is valuable.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
intermediate
15 min
Items Exempt from Hadd for Theft: Lack of Hiraz and Shubha
List of items immune from cutting due to lack of proper safeguarding or legal doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the items exempt from hadd for theft in Hanafi fiqh and explain the two main reasons (lack of hiraz and presence of shubha) that justify the exemption.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the hand is not cut for items whose theft can be plausibly excused — lack of secure storage or a legal doubt negates the full criminal intent.

The hadd penalty for theft is severe, but it is not applied mechanically. Hanafi fiqh carefully considers the circumstances and the nature of the stolen item. In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author lists specific items for which the hand is not cut, either because they are not properly safeguarded (hiraz) or because the thief might have a plausible excuse (shubha). Understanding these exemptions is crucial for correctly applying the law of theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main grounds for exemption
Read the passage and note the recurring reasons: lack of proper storage (عدم الحرز) and legal doubt/plausible excuse (شبهة / تأول). These are the pillars of the exemption.
2
Classify items by ground
Sort the listed items into two categories: those exempt due to no hiraz (e.g., fruit on tree, unharvested crop, mosque door) and those exempt due to shubha/ta'wil (e.g., mus-haf, gold cross, musical instruments, notebooks). Some items may combine both.
Note that the same item may be exempt for multiple reasons.
3
Identify where the hadd does apply
The passage specifies exceptions: harvested and stored crop, drinks that remain for a year, small slave who cannot express himself, accounting notebook. In these cases, the hand is cut if nisab is reached.
This shows that the exemption is not absolute; it depends on conditions.
4
Analyze the role of ta'wil (claim of lawful intent)
For items like mus-haf, cross, and musical instruments, the thief could plausibly claim a lawful purpose (reading, breaking evil, forbidding wrong). This creates a shubha that blocks the hadd.
The Hanafi school applies this broadly; other schools require the claim to be credible or proven.
5
Compare with other schools
Note that the three other Imams (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) generally hold that the hand is cut in these cases. Understand their reasoning: they see these items as property and do not accept the shubha as sufficient to avert the hadd.
This illustrates a key methodological difference in the treatment of shubha.

(مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وطنبور : [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في طنبور وبقية آلات الملاهي. (عيني)] أي لا قطع في سرقة طنبور – بضم الطاء وكذا في طبل ودف ومزمار وبربط كما يأتي؛ لأنه يتأول السارق فيها الإنكار والأمر بالمعروف، وعند بعض الشافعية يقطع في دف وأخواته من آلات اللهو إذا بلغ نصاباً بعد نقص ماليته، ولو سرق طبلاً للغزاة، وهو يساوي عشرة تكلموا فيه، والمختار أنه لا يقطع فيه؛ لأنه كما يصلح للغزاة يصلح للهو فتمكنت الشبهة [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٣/١] (فتح) ومصحف: [ أي وكذا لا يقطع في مصحف ولو كان محلّى بحلية، ولو لم يحسن القراءة. (عيني، ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة مصحف ولو كان المصحف محلى بحلية قدر النصب، وقال الشافعي: يقطع؛ لأنه مال متقوم، حتى يجوز بيعه، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، وهو رواية عن أبي يوسف، وعنه أيضاً أنه يقطع إذا كانت حليته نصاباً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] لأنها ليست من المصحف فتعتبر بانفرادها ولنا أن أخذ المصحف يتأول فيه القراءة والنظر، والحلية تبع له ولا عبرة للتبع كمن سرق الخمر في إناء، وقيمة الإناء مقدار النصاب أو أكثر. (ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة باب مسجد؛ لعدم الإحراز، فإن قلت: الحكم في باب الدار هكذا فما الفائدة في تخصيص المسجد؟ قلت: إنما خص به لمناسبة المصحف وإنما لا يقطع بسرقة باب مركب؛ لأنه حرز لا محرز، لكن لو اعتاد سرقة أبواب المساجد بولغ في تعزيره وحبسه حتى يتوب، والمراد بالمركب ما كان خارج البيت فلو كان مركباً داخل الحرز قطع. وصليب ذهب، وشطرنج ونرد، وصبي حرّ ولو معه حلي، وعبد كبير ودفاتر، وكذا لا يقطع فيه

لأنه غصب
وصليب ذهب : أي مطلقاً سواء كان في المصلى أو في غيره، وقيد الذهب اتفاقي؛ لأن الحكم لا يختلف فيما إذا الفضة، ويقطع عند الشافعي وبعض الحنابلة، وعن أبي يوسف أن صليب الذهب إذا كان في المصلى – أي موضع صلاتهم - لا يقطع، وإن كان في بيت آخر يقطع ووجه عدم القطع، تأول الكسر نهياً عن المنكر. رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] (معدن، فتح) وشطرنج ونرد أي ولو من ذهب والشطرنج بكسر الشين واختلف في لفظه هل هو عربي أو معرب؟ فقيل: إنه عربي، ويقرأ بالسين المهملة والمعجمة، وقال الحريري: الشطرنج بفتح الشين، وهو معرب إلا أنه بكسر الشين في استعمال العرب، وقوله: "ونرد" بفتح النون، وهو الذي تلعبه الإفرنج ولو من ذهب؛ لتأول السارق الكسر والنهي عن المنكر، ولو سرق دراهم عليها تمثال قطع؛ لأنه أعد للتمول فلا يثبت فيه تأويل، وفي الشطرنج والنرد يقطع عند الشافعي وبعض الحنابلة (عيني (فتح) وصبي حر وكذا لا يقطع في صبي. (عيني) وصبي حر إلخ: الحلي بفتح الحاء وسكون اللام كظي، وجمعه حلي بضم الحاء وكسر اللام وتشديد الياء، أي قة لا قطع في سر صبي حر؛ لأنه ليس بمال والحلي تبع له ولأنه يتأول في أخذه الحمل إلى منزله، وقال أبو يوسف: يقطع إذا كان عليه حلي يبلغ النصاب، وبه قالت الثلاثة، والخلاف في غير المميز الذي لا يمشي ولا يتكلم حتى لا يكون في يد نفسه ولو كان يمشي ويتكلم لا يقطع إجماعاً؛ لأن له يداً على نفسه وما عليه تابع له فكان أخذه خداعاً لا سرقة. (مستخلص، مسكين، عيني) وعبد كبير أي ولا يقطع في عبد مميز ولو نائماً أو مجنوناً أو أعجمياً؛ لأنه غصب لا سرقة. (عيني، ط)
و دفاتر [ أي لا يقطع فيها إن كانت لغير الحساب سواء كانت شرعية أو لا. (ط)] أي لا قطع في سرقة دفاتر كلها؛ لأن المقصود ما فيها، وذلك ليس بمال والمراد من الدفاتر صحائف فيها كتابة من عربية أو شعر أو حديث أو تفسير وغير ذلك، ولا قطع فيها؛ لأنها إن كانت شرعية كتب الفقه والحديث والتفسير فهي كالمصحف، وإن كان شيئاً مكروها فهي كالطنبور، ولو سرق الكواغذ والجلود قبل الكتابة يقطع إن بلغ نصاباً، وفي دفاتر الأدب روايتان، في رواية ملحقة بالحساب وفي رواية ملحقة بالأحاديث والتفسير. رمز الحقائق: ٤٠٤/١] (مستخلص) بخلاف الصغير: [ أي بخلاف سرقة العبد الصغير الذي لا يعبر عن نفسه. (عيني)، وبخلاف دفتر الحساب التي مضى حسابها فيقطع إن بلغت نصاباً. (ط)] فإنه يقطع فيهما؛ لأن السرقة في العبد الصغير متحقق؛ لأنه لا يد له على نفسه . أنه مال فصار كالدواب، وقال أبو يوسف: لا يقطع فيه كما في الكبير والمميز؛ لأنه آدمي وإن كان مالاً وإن كانت قيمته أقل من : نصاب وفي أذنه مثله شيء يقطع باعتبار الضم، وأما القطع في دفتر الحساب؛ لأن ما فيها مع من وجه يقصد بالأخذ فكان المقصود هو الكواغذ فيقطع إذا بلغت نصاباً، وعند الثلاثة: يقطع في الكل. (مستخلص، عيني)
The Book of Theft. Rulings of Theft. Or [theft of] fruit on a tree, milk, meat, watermelon, unharvested crop, intoxicating drinks, tanbur, and everything that does not remain for a year, and a mus-haf even if embellished, and a mosque door. That is: or fruit on a tree due to lack of safeguarding. And milk and meat: i.e., the hand is not cut for stealing milk and meat even if dried, nor for every food prepared for eating like bread, and in times of famine there is no cutting for stealing food due to necessity, and it has come in the hadith of Umar: 'No cutting in a year of famine, nor for a suspended date bunch.' Hence Al-Muntaqa did not differentiate between food and other things in famine because it is permitted to eat without price in times of scarcity without distinction between food prepared for eating and otherwise. And crop: i.e., the hand is not cut for stealing unharvested crop, and it is restricted by not being harvested because if harvested and stored in non-famine times, the hand is cut. According to the three [Imams], the hand is cut in all these cases. Unharvested: i.e., likewise the hand is not cut for crop due to lack of storage. And drinks: whether intoxicating or non-intoxicating even if the container is gold. i.e., the hand is not cut for stealing drinks absolutely, whether sweet or bitter, and the intended meaning of drinks is those that do not remain and quickly spoil, whether intoxicating or not, contrary to vinegar and honey, for the hand is cut in them by consensus. The reason for no cutting in drinks is that the thief may claim he took them to pour away, and because some of them are not property like wine, and some have reduced value like musaffa and badhiq, thus doubt of non-property is established. As for drinks that remain and are stored for a year, most are property by consensus, so the hand is cut. And tanbur: i.e., likewise the hand is not cut for a tanbur and the rest of musical instruments. i.e., no cutting for stealing tanbur – with damm on the ta – and similarly drum, tambourine, flute, and rubab as will come, because the thief may claim he was forbidding evil and enjoining good. According to some Shafi'is, the hand is cut for a tambourine and similar instruments of entertainment if it reaches nisab after reduction of value. If he steals a drum for fighters and it is worth ten, they discussed it; the chosen view is that the hand is not cut because just as it is suitable for fighters, it is also suitable for entertainment, so doubt is established. And mus-haf: i.e., likewise the hand is not cut for a mus-haf even if embellished with ornamentation, even if he does not know how to read. i.e., no cutting for stealing a mus-haf even if the mus-haf is ornamented with gold worth nisab. Al-Shafi'i said: the hand is cut because it is valuable property such that its sale is permissible, and Malik and Ahmad said the same, and there is a narration from Abu Yusuf, and also from him that the hand is cut if its ornamentation reaches nisab because it is not part of the mus-haf so it is considered independently. Our proof: the taking of a mus-haf may be interpreted as intending to read and look at it, and the ornamentation is subsidiary to it, and subsidiary is not considered, like one who steals wine in a container where the container's value is nisab or more. And mosque door: i.e., likewise the hand is not cut for a mosque door and a house because it is a safe but not safeguarded, and the hand is not cut for the furnishings of the Ka'bah. i.e., no cutting for stealing a mosque door due to lack of safeguarding. If you say: the ruling for a house door is likewise, so what is the benefit of specifying the mosque? I say: it is specified because of appropriateness with the mus-haf, and the hand is not cut for stealing a door that is hung because it is a safe but not safeguarded, but if he habitually steals mosque doors, he is severely disciplined and imprisoned until he repents. The intended meaning of 'hung' is what is outside the house; if it is hung inside a safe, the hand is cut. The mosque and privacy. And a gold cross, chess and backgammon, a free child even if he has jewelry, a mature slave, and notebooks – likewise the hand is not cut in these. With kasr on the shin. Contrary to the small [slave] and the accounting notebook. If the cross is of gold? That is, if the child has jewelry with fath on the ha and sukun on the lam, even if of gold. Because it is usurpation. And a gold cross: i.e., absolutely whether in a place of prayer or elsewhere, and the restriction of gold is incidental because the ruling does not differ if it is silver. The hand is cut according to al-Shafi'i and some Hanbalis. From Abu Yusuf: if a gold cross is in a place of prayer – i.e., their prayer place – the hand is not cut, but if in another house, the hand is cut. The reason for no cutting is the claim of breaking it as forbidding evil. And chess and backgammon: i.e., even if of gold. 'Chess' with kasr on the shin; there is dispute whether it is Arabic or borrowed. It is said it is Arabic and read with sin and shin. Al-Hariri said: chess with fath on the shin, it is borrowed, but with kasr in Arab usage. His saying: 'and backgammon' with fath on the nun, which the Franks play, even if of gold, because the thief may claim he is breaking and forbidding evil. If he steals dirhams with an image on them, the hand is cut because they are prepared for wealth so no claim is established. In chess and backgammon, the hand is cut according to al-Shafi'i and some Hanbalis. And a free child: likewise the hand is not cut for a child. i.e., no cutting for stealing a free child because it is not property, and the jewelry is subsidiary to him, and because he may claim he took him to carry to his home. Abu Yusuf said: the hand is cut if he has jewelry reaching nisab, and the three [Imams] said the same. The dispute is regarding a non-discerning child who does not walk or talk so that he is not in his own possession. If he walks and talks, the hand is not cut by consensus because he has possession of himself and what is on him is subsidiary to him, so taking him is trickery, not theft. And a mature slave: i.e., the hand is not cut for a discerning slave even if sleeping, insane, or non-Arab, because it is usurpation, not theft. And notebooks: i.e., the hand is not cut in them if they are not for accounting, whether religious or not. i.e., no cutting for stealing all notebooks because the intended purpose is their content, which is not property. The intended meaning of notebooks is pages containing writing – Arabic, poetry, hadith, tafsir, etc. There is no cutting in them because if they are religious books of fiqh, hadith, tafsir, they are like the mus-haf; if it is something disliked, they are like the tanbur. If he steals paper and leather before writing, the hand is cut if it reaches nisab. Regarding literary notebooks, there are two narrations: in one narration they are joined to accounting notebooks, in another to hadith and tafsir. Contrary to the small: i.e., contrary to stealing a small slave who cannot express himself, and contrary to an accounting notebook whose accounts have been settled – the hand is cut in both if they reach nisab. Because theft in a small slave is verified as he has no possession of himself and is property, like animals. Abu Yusuf said: the hand is not cut in it as in the mature and discerning because he is a human even if property, even if his value is less than nisab and in his ear is something worth nisab – the hand is cut by considering the combined value. As for cutting in an accounting notebook, because what is in it in some respects is intended taking, so the intended thing is the papers, so the hand is cut if they reach nisab. According to the three [Imams], the hand is cut in all.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq enumerates items for which the hadd penalty of hand-cutting is not applied in Hanafi fiqh. The commentary expands on the reasons, which center on lack of proper safeguarding (hiraz) or existence of a legal doubt (shubha) that negates the full criminal intent. The items include fruit on trees, milk, meat, watermelon, unharvested crops, intoxicating drinks (except those that are stored and have lasting value like vinegar and honey), musical instruments like tanbur and drum, a mus-haf (Quran manuscript) even if ornamented, mosque doors, gold crosses, chess and backgammon sets, a free child (even if wearing jewelry), a mature slave, and notebooks containing writing. The rulings are justified by several principles: (1) lack of hiraz – the item is not in a secure place; (2) the thief may have a plausible excuse (ta'wil), such as intending to read the mus-haf, pour away wine, break a cross, or forbid evil by taking a musical instrument; (3) some items are not considered property in the full sense (e.g., wine is not mal mutaqawwim for Muslims). The passage also records disagreements with the three other Sunni schools (al-Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) who generally cut the hand in these cases, and internal Hanafi differences, notably Abu Yusuf's opinion that ornaments on a child or mus-haf may be considered independently. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is the one stated: no cutting for these items. Students should note that the exemption does not mean the act is permissible; the thief is still liable for compensation and may face discretionary punishment (ta'zir), especially if habitual.

Key Terms
حرز (hiraz): A secure place where property is stored; theft from a place that is not a proper hiraz does not incur the hadd penalty.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of the hadd penalty. Here, the thief's plausible excuse (like intending to read the mus-haf) creates a shubha.
تأول (ta'wil): An interpretation or excuse claimed by the thief that his action had a lawful purpose, even if mistaken. It creates a shubha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school exempt the theft of a mus-haf (Quran manuscript) from the hadd penalty, even if it is ornamented with gold?
A) Because a mus-haf is not considered property.
B) Because the thief may have intended to read or look at it, creating a legal doubt.
C) Because it is not kept in a secure place (hiraz).
D) Because its value is less than the nisab.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the hadd for theft is not applied when the item lacks proper safeguarding or when the thief can plausibly claim a lawful intent, reflecting the principle that hudud are averted by doubts.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties

Theft of a Gold Cross: Shubhah and the Dropping of Hadd
Ruling on theft of a gold cross with conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of a cross, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Yusuf vs. the Imamayn), and evaluate the role of ta'awwul al-kasr as a shubhah.

Core Idea
Theft of a cross: no hadd because the thief may claim to break it; shubhah averts punishment.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the law of theft (sariqah) where the object stolen is a cross, a symbol of disbelief. The Hanafi school holds that the amputation penalty is not applied due to a plausible claim of religious intent. We will explore the reasoning, the dispute within the Hanafi school between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other, and the contrasting view of the Shafi‘i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its object
Note that the passage lists items for which hadd is not applied, including a gold cross. The ruling is absolute: no amputation for theft of a cross, whether gold or silver, from any location.
The mention of gold is incidental; the same applies to silver.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta‘awwul al-kasr)
The key proof (wajh) is that the thief can plausibly claim an intention to break the cross as a religious duty, creating a shubhah (legal doubt) that drops the hadd.
This is based on the principle that hadd is averted by shubhah.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf distinguishes by location: if stolen from a church (musalla), no cut; if from a private home, cut. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad maintain no cut in either case.
Abu Yusuf limits the shubhah to contexts where the intention is more plausible.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi‘i school and some Hanbalis apply the hadd because the cross is valuable property and the taking itself is theft; they do not accept the ta‘awwul as a valid shubhah.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is clear: Hanafi: no cut; Shafi‘i: cut.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that no amputation is applied in any case, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Imam al-Marghinani and other later authorities affirm this as the preferred view.

And a golden cross, and chess and backgammon, and a free boy even if with him is jewelry, and a mature slave and notebooks, and likewise no amputation is applied therein. ... 'A golden cross': i.e., absolutely whether it is in the place of prayer or elsewhere, and the specification of gold is incidental because the ruling does not differ in the case of silver. And amputation is applied according to al-Shafi'i and some Hanbalis. And from Abu Yusuf: that a golden cross if it is in the place of prayer—i.e., their place of worship—no amputation, but if it is in another house, amputation. And the reason for non-amputation is that the thief interprets the break as prohibiting evil.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the theft of a golden cross. The text begins by listing items for which the hadd (hand amputation) is not applied. The author clarifies that the cross is considered absolutely, whether it is in a church (musalla) or elsewhere, and that the mention of gold is incidental—the same applies to silver. The Hanafi position is that no hadd is imposed because the thief can plausibly claim he intended to break the cross as it is a symbol of disbelief (ta'awwul al-kasr). However, the Shafi'i school and some Hanbalis hold that amputation is applied. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Yusuf makes a distinction: if the cross is stolen from a place of worship (musalla), the ta'awwul is valid, so no cut; but if from a private home, the ta'awwul is less plausible, so amputation applies. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that no cut applies in either case, as per Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad. The reasoning is based on the principle that wherever there is a plausible legal interpretation (ta'awwul), the hadd is averted.

Key Terms
تأول الكسر (ta'awwul al-kasr): The thief's plausible claim that he intended to break the cross (as a religious duty) rather than steal it.
صليب (salib): A cross, considered an object of kufr.

Theft of a cross does not incur hadd in the Hanafi school due to the plausible claim of intent to break it, illustrating the principle that shubhah averts punishment.

Theft of Chess and Backgammon: The Hanafi Ruling on Hadd
Ruling on non-application of hadd for certain items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on hadd for stealing chess/backgammon sets and explain the role of shubhah (ta'awwul) in dropping the penalty.

Core Idea
No hadd on chess and backgammon because the thief can claim he intended to break them.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a nuanced issue in the law of theft (sariqa): whether the hadd penalty applies to stealing chess and backgammon sets, even if made of gold. The Hanafi school rules that no amputation is required, grounded in the principle that hadd is averted by doubts (shubhah). The thief's potential claim that he took the items to destroy them as prohibited instruments creates a legitimate doubt that blocks the penalty. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with items like dirhams bearing images, where no such doubt exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and the ruling
The passage states that chess (shatranj) and backgammon (nard), even if made of gold, do not incur the hadd penalty for theft.
This is the Hanafi position.
2
Understand the reason: ta'awwul al-kasr
The thief can claim he took the items to break them, as they are instruments of prohibited amusement. This creates a shubhah (doubt) that prevents the application of hadd.
The principle 'Avert hudud by doubts' is the basis.
3
Distinguish from dirhams with images
Dirhams with images are prepared for tamawwul (wealth), so no such ta'awwul exists. The thief cannot claim he intended to destroy them; thus hadd applies.
Key distinction: the intended use of the item.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i and some Hanbalis hold that hadd applies, considering chess/backgammon as valuable property like any other.
The difference hinges on whether the shubhah is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is said it is Arabic, and it is read with sin and shin. Al-Hariri said: chess with fatha on the shin, and it is a loanword except that the Arabs use it with kasra on the shin. Commentary
This passage continues the list of items for which the hadd is not applied: chess and backgammon sets, even if made of gold. The author remarks on the etymology of 'shatranj' and 'nard', noting that chess is used with a kasra on the shin in Arabic. The ruling is that no amputation applies because the thief can claim he intended to break the chess or backgammon set as a prohibited amusement (ta'awwul al-kasr). This is in contrast to dirhams that have images (like coins with figures), where amputation is applied because such coins are meant for wealth (tamawwul), so no ta'awwul is accepted. The Shafi'i school and some Hanbalis hold that amputation applies even for chess/backgammon. The Hanafi position is based on the shubhah created by the possibility of the thief breaking the items.

Key Terms
الشطرنج (al-shatranj): Chess, considered an instrument of amusement.
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon, similarly an amusement tool.
التأويل (al-ta'wīl): Interpretation; in this context, the thief's claim that he intended to break the items.
التمول (al-tamawwul): Treating something as wealth; items prepared for monetary use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

No Hadd for Stealing a Free Boy or a Mature Slave
Ruling on theft of a free boy and a mature slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stealing a free boy and a mature slave, explain the reasoning, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a free boy with jewelry.

Core Idea
A free boy and a mature slave are not property — taking them is not sariqah meriting hadd, but ghasp or deception.

This lesson addresses two specific cases where the hadd punishment for theft is not applied despite apparent property being taken: the theft of a free boy and the theft of a mature slave. Understanding the reasoning behind these exceptions is crucial to grasping the strict conditions for sariqah in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories and the default ruling
Recognize that the source discusses two distinct cases: stealing a free boy (even with jewelry) and stealing a mature slave. The default ruling for both is that no hadd punishment is applied.
2
Understand the reasoning for the free boy
The free boy is not considered property (mal). His jewelry is subsidiary (tabi') to him. Additionally, the thief may plausibly claim he intended to carry the boy home (ta'awwul), making the act deception, not theft.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the free boy with jewelry
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if the boy wears jewelry worth nisab, amputation applies because the jewelry is property. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and later Hanafi scholars) reject this view. Note the consensus on a discerning boy (no cut) and the dispute only for a non-discerning boy.
The dispute is only when the boy is non-discerning (cannot walk or talk).
4
Apply the ruling to the mature slave
A mature (discerning) slave, even if sleeping, insane, or non-Arabic speaking, is not considered property in the sense of sariqah. Taking him is ghasp (usurpation), not theft, so no hadd.

Source Text

Theft of Notebooks and Account Books in Hanafi Hudud
Ruling on theft of notebooks and account books

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft of notebooks based on their content, distinguish when amputation applies for blank paper, account books, and small slaves, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the small slave.

Core Idea
In Hanafi hudud, the object's intended use determines amputation: no cut for written content, cut for the material itself.

Theft of notebooks (dafatir) raises a nuanced question: does the hadd of amputation apply? The answer hinges on whether the stolen item's value lies in its physical material or in the writing it contains. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of notebooks—religious, literary, blank, and account books—and contrasts with the small slave. You will also encounter an intra-Hanafi disagreement (Abu Yusuf vs. the majority) and the position of the three other imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Object Categories
List the distinct objects mentioned: notebooks with religious writing, literary notebooks, blank paper/leather, account books, and small slave. Note that each has a separate ruling.
The passage uses 'دَفَاتِر' generically, then specifies subcategories.
2
Determine the Ruling for Each
From the text: no cut for religious notebooks (like mushaf) or secular writing (like tanbur); no cut for literary notebooks according to the stronger narration, but cut in the other narration. Blank paper/leather: cut if nisab. Account book: cut if nisab. Small slave: cut (majority view), but Abu Yusuf says no cut.
The ruling is not uniform; it depends on whether the value is in the physical substance or the intellectual content.
3
Understand the Talil (Reasoning)
The reasoning (talil) is that for non-account notebooks, the goal is the writing, which is not property (mal). For blank paper, the goal is the paper itself (property). For account books, the paper is what is taken, even if the accounts are the motive—the paper is the object of theft. For the small slave, he has no hand over himself, so he is like property (similar to animals), thus theft is established.
Note that the 'goals' (maqasid) of the thief matter in defining what is stolen.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees on the small slave, arguing that the slave is human, so no cut (like an adult slave). There are also two narrations on literary notebooks. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) apply cut in all cases, showing cross-madhhab divergence.
The mufta bihi (operative view) in the Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on the small slave (cut), and the narration equating literary notebooks with account books (i.e., cut) is considered weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And notebooks: no amputation is applied therein if they are not account books, whether religious or not. The meaning of notebooks is pages containing writing of Arabic poetry, hadith, tafsir, etc. There is no cut because if they are religious books (fiqh, hadith, tafsir), they are like the mushaf; and if they are disliked things, they are like the tanbur.
