Commentary
This passage discusses notebooks (dafatir) and distinguishes between different types. The ruling is that no hadd is applied for stealing notebooks that are not account books, whether they contain religious writings (fiqh, hadith, tafsir) or secular poetry. Religious writings are like the mushaf (Quran) in sanctity, and secular writing like a tanbur (musical instrument) in being of little value. However, if the paper and leather are stolen before writing, then hadd applies if they reach nisab. For literary notebooks, there are two narrations: one equating them to account books (thus cutting) and one to religious writings (thus no cut). The text then contrasts with the small slave (saghir) and account notebook (daftar al-hisab), where amputation is applied. For the small slave who cannot express himself, theft is established because he has no hand over himself and is considered like property. Abu Yusuf disagrees and says no cut, similar to the mature slave. For the account book, the intended thing is the paper itself (since the accounts are what matter), so cut applies if nisab is met. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) apply cut in all cases.

دفتر الحساب (daftar al-hisab): Account book; the paper is the intended object, so cut applies.
صغير (saghir): Small slave (child) who cannot express himself; treated as property for theft.
الثلاثة (al-thalatha): The three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) who apply cut in all notebook types.

Key Takeaway
Amputation for theft depends on whether the stolen object is itself valuable (like blank paper) or if its value lies in the content (like written notebooks); the Hanafi school carefully distinguishes based on the intended object of theft.

Theft from Public Treasury and Joint Property: Ruling on Hadd
Ruling on theft from public or joint property with ta'lil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on theft from public treasury and jointly owned property, and explain why shubhah averts the hadd penalty.

Core Idea
No hand cut when the thief has a share in the stolen property.

This lesson addresses a key exception in Hanafi hudud law: the hadd penalty is not applied for theft from the public treasury (bayt al-mal) or jointly owned property. The rationale is that the thief has a right in the property, creating a legal doubt (shubhah) about the victim's exclusive ownership. Understanding this exception clarifies the strict conditions required for the application of hadd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text discusses two specific theft scenarios: from public treasury and from jointly owned property. The ruling is identical: no hadd (no hand cut).
This is a ruling of non-application, not an act's obligation.
2
Understand the ta'lil (rationale)
The text provides the reason: because the thief is part of the public (for public wealth) or has a share (for joint property), creating a shubhah. The thief's own right in the property negates the condition of exclusive ownership required for hadd.
Shubhah is a legal doubt that averts the hadd, based on the Prophetic principle 'Idra'u al-hudud bi al-shubuhab'.
3
Apply the underlying principle
Generalize: any case where the stolen property is not exclusively owned by the victim, due to the thief's share, drops the hadd. This includes public funds and co-owned items.
The principle is broader than these two examples.
4
Note cross-madhhab difference
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may apply hadd if the thief's share is negligible or if public property is considered owned by the state. The Hanafi position is definitive on this exception.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومال عامة إلخ: أي لا قطع في سرقة مال العامة أي في بيت المال؛ لأنه حق العامة والسارق منهم، فأورث الشبهة، وقوله: "أو مشتركة" أي لا قطع في سرقة مال مشترك بين السارق والمسروق منه؛ لأن للسارق فيه حقاً فأورث الشبهة.
And wealth of the public etc.: i.e., no cut in stealing the wealth of the public, i.e., from the treasury, because it is the right of the public and the thief is among them, so it creates shubhah. And his saying: 'or joint [property]': i.e., no cut in stealing property jointly owned between the thief and the victim, because the thief has a right in it, so it creates shubhah.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage addresses theft from public treasury (bayt al-mal) and jointly owned property. For public wealth, no hadd is applied because the thief himself is part of the public and has a share in that wealth. This creates a shubhah (doubt) regarding the complete ownership of the victim, and shubhah drops the hadd. Similarly, for jointly owned property between the thief and the victim, the thief has a right in the property, so it is not fully the victim's exclusive property. This also creates shubhah. The principle is that for hadd to be applied, the property must be exclusively owned by the victim; any co-ownership by the thief prevents the hadd.

Key Terms
مال العامة (mal al-ammah): Public treasury (bayt al-mal); property belonging to the Muslim community.
مال مشترك (mal mushtarak): Jointly owned property between the thief and the victim.
شبهة (shubhah): A legal doubt or ambiguity that averts the application of hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the hadd of theft not applied when the stolen property is jointly owned by the thief and the victim?
A) Because the property is not considered protected (hirz).
B) Because the thief has a right in the property, creating a doubt about exclusive ownership.
C) Because the theft did not meet the minimum nisab.
D) Because the victim's share is unclear.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not impose the hadd penalty for theft if the stolen property is not exclusively owned by the victim, as the thief's own right creates a shubhah.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
beginner
6 min
Acts Excluded from Hadd: Breach of Trust, Plunder, Snatching
Ruling on three non-theft takings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyanah, nahb, and ikhtilas regarding hadd and explain why they are not sariqah.

Core Idea
No amputation for breach of trust, plunder, or snatching – they lack the secrecy of sariqah.

Theft (sariqah) in Hanafi fiqh is a specific crime with strict conditions. Not every unauthorized taking of property qualifies for the severe hadd penalty. This lesson examines three acts that are explicitly excluded: khiyanah (breach of trust by a depositary), nahb (plunder by open force), and ikhtilas (snatching from hand or house). Understanding these exclusions sharpens your grasp of the precise definition of sariqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts
Name and define each: khiyanah, nahb, ikhtilas. Note how they differ from sariqah.
These are distinct from stealing from a guarded place.
2
State the ruling
For each act, state the hukm: no hadd (amputation) applies.
3
Cite the dalil
Provide the hadith text: 'There is no cutting for a traitor, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher.' Note its transmission chain (Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi) and that it is authenticated by al-Tirmidhi.
This is an ahad hadith, but the meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
4
Explain the reasoning
Analyze why each lacks the condition of sariqah: khiyanah has deficiency in hirz (the depositary was trusted with the item), nahb is open not secret, ikhtilas is also not secret since it is taken from the hand or house openly.
The condition of sariqah is taking secretly from a guarded place (hirz).
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, determine if it is sariqah or one of these excluded acts, and state the consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبخيانة إلخ : أي ولا يقطع بخيانة المودع ما في يده من الشيء المأمون، ولا قطع بنهب: وهو الأخذ علانية على وجه القهر، ولا قطع بالاختلاس وهو أن يختطف الشيء من يد المالك أو من البيت؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس على خائن ولا منتهب ولا مختلس قطع"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود وغيرهما، وصححه الترمذي؛ ولأن في الخيانة قصور في الحرز وفي النهب لعدم الشرط وهو الأخذ خفية، وكذا في الاختلاس.
And breach of trust etc.: i.e., no amputation is applied for breach of trust of the depositary regarding what is in his hand of the trustworthy item. And no cut for plunder: which is taking openly by force. And no cut for snatching: which is to snatch something from the hand of the owner or from the house. Due to his statement (peace be upon him): "There is no cutting for a traitor, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher." Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud and others, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi. And because in breach of trust there is deficiency in guarding (al-hirz), and in plunder the condition of taking secretly is absent, and similarly in snatching.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage deals with three acts that are not considered sariqah (theft) for the purpose of hadd: khiyanah (breach of trust), nahb (plunder), and ikhtilas (snatching). The proof is a hadith: "There is no cutting for a traitor, nor for a plunderer, nor for a snatcher." The reasoning is that breach of trust occurs when a person entrusted with property (like a depositary) takes it; this lacks the element of proper guarding (hirz). Plunder is open taking by force, not secret. Snatching is also not secret, as it is done from the hand of the owner or from a house quickly. All three miss the condition of sariqah: secret taking from a guarded place. Therefore, no hadd applies.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Breach of trust by a depositary or trustee.
نهب (nahb): Plunder: taking property openly by force.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): Snatching: taking quickly from hand or house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason that no hadd applies to snatching (ikhtilas) in the Hanafi school?
A) The value of the snatched item is usually low.
B) The taking is done openly, not secretly.
C) The owner is always present during the act.
D) Snatching is considered a form of khiyanah.

Key Takeaway
Breach of trust, plunder, and snatching are not sariqah; they do not incur hadd because they lack the condition of secret taking from a guarded place.
